Abstract  
The market for the antioxidant beta-carotene is currently increasing, partly due to the status as a natural or-
ange pigment and partly due to the potential of conversion into high value carotenoids. To fulfil the market, 
the production capacity needs to be increased, for instance by establishing new production platforms. The 
aim of this study was to characterise and optimise the production by one these platforms – the engineered 
yeast strain Yarrowia lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. All media optimisation strategies were based on increasing the 
pool of precursors for the mevalonate pathway, where beta-carotene is synthesised. The strategies included 
investigating the effect of; pH-value, carbon/nitrogen ratio, carbon source, complex media components and 
oleic acid. The medium was based on the minimal medium CBS/Delft. Media optimisation experiments were 
carried out in the BioLector® I high-throughput micro-fermentation system. For comparison of six optimisa-
tion parameters (specific growth rate (µ), final biomass concentration the yield Ysx, beta-carotene concentra-
tion, the yield Ysp and the yield Yxp) an experiment with standard CBS-glucose medium at the standard condi-
tions was used as the baseline.  
The experiments showed that the optima were: pH 7, carbon/nitrogen ratio 0.875 [moleC/moleN],1 vol% of 
oleic acid and glycerol and the primary carbon source. It could be concluded that a medium with the optima 
combined, had  significantly higher values for specific growth rate, final biomass concentration and Ysx than 
the baseline experiment. For beta-carotene concentration, Ysx and Ypx  there was no significant difference 
between the optimised medium and the baseline.  
The thesis contains suggestions to further improvement of the strain as well as the medium 
 
Resume 
Markedet for antioxidanten, beta-karoten er i vækst, og udsigter viser, at det vil fortsætte med at stige. Der 
er flere grunde til stigningen; dels, at beta-karoen er et naturligt pigment i fødevareindustrien, og dels fordi 
andre højværdikartenoider kan syntetiseres fra beta-karoten. For at kunne følge med den stigende efter-
spørgsel er der behov for nye produktionsplatforme, som kan opretholde produktionskapaciteten. For ek-
semplel i form of heterologe cellefabrikker. Målet med dette studie var at karakterisere og optimere beta-
karoten produktionen af en af disse cellefabrikker – den rekombinante gær Yarrowia lipolytica  ob-
CHCTEFCTEF. De medieoptimerings startegier, der blev brugt i dette studie sigtede alle efter at øge mængden 
af byggeblokke, der kan syntetises til beta-karoten. En række variable blev testet, for at bestemme deres op-
timum baseret på seks parametre: den specifikke vækstrate (µ), den endelige biomassekoncentration, ud-
byttekoeficienten Ysx, den endelige beta-karoenkoncentration samt udbyttekoeficienterne Ysp og Yxp. De te-
stede variable var: pH-værdi, forholdet mellem kulstof og nitrogen, kulstofkilde samt effekten of komplekse 
mediekomponenter og tilsætningen af oliesyre. Resultatrene blev sammenlignet med værdierne fra en fer-
mentering i CBS-medie under standard betingelser (udgangspunkt). Alle variable blev testet i mikrofermen-
teringssystemet BioLector® I.  
Det eksperimentelle arbejde viste, at optima var: pH 7, et forhold mellem kulstof og nitrogen på 0.875 
[mol/mol], 1vol% oliesyre og glycerol som den primære kuldtofkilde. Det kunne herefter konkluderes at et 
media med disse optima var signifikant bedre, end udgangspunktetet, baseret på den specifikke vækst rate, 
den endelige biomasse koncentration og udbyttekoeficienten Ysx. Det var dog ikke muligt at drage en signifi-
kant konklusion baseret på den endelige beta-karoen koncentration samt udbyttekoeficienterne Ysp og Yxp.  
Dette speciale indeholder også strategier til yderligere optimering, både baseret på stamme- og medieopti-
mering.

Two three-week extensions were given to the author. The project period was therefore 6.5 months rather 
than 5 months. The first extension was given from the DTU Bioengineering Study Board, as the author was 
requested to instruct a course at the bachelor’s level for full time in three weeks. The second extension was 
given by the DTU Dispensation board, based on technical difficulties as a crucial piece of equipment was un-
available for ten weeks due to repairs.  
 
The appendix section of this thesis contains the most vital graphs, tables and calculations to fully understand 
the content of this thesis. All other data (“Raw data”) is in the Cloud and can be accessed by any holder of 
the weblink provided in Appendix 22.  
 
The vast majority of the figures in this thesis are made by the author, if the figure is adapted from scientific 
work or inspired by scientists this is clearly stated in the legend with a reference.

1. Introduction  
Beta-carotene is an orange-coloured tetraterpenoid produced in many plants and other eukaryotic organ-
isms. Due to its potent and bright orange colour, beta-carotene is popular in the food industry as a natural 
pigment. The beautiful colour is not the only relevant feature; beta-carotene is a component in the human 
eye, therefore, it prevents blindness. Furthermore, beta-carotene is an antioxidant, which means theta it 
eliminates reactive oxygen species (ROS) which would otherwise cause damage to DNA and RNA along with 
deactivation of enzymes (Ribeiro, Barreto, and Coelho 2011). Other high value carotenoids have beta-caro-
tene as precursor (Kildegaard et al. 2017). The many uses of carotenoids have led to a total market size of 
1.4 billion American dollars this year, the market of beta-carotene alone accounted for 432.2 million Ameri-
can dollars in 2015. As the interest in beta-carotene is increasing, the demand will also increase over the 
coming years. The market size for beta-carotene is therefore estimated to reach 600 million American dol-
lars in 2024 (Grand View Resarch 2016; Larroude et al. 2017). To satisfy the market, beta-carotene is pro-
duced in several ways; chemical synthesis from traditional chemicals, biotechnological production by natural 
producers and recombinant hosts. Chemical synthesis is responsible for the largest part of the production.  
 
Besides chemical synthesis, the main industrial producer is the algae Dunaliella salina, which is cultivated in 
large open tanks in sodium-chloride rich water; its beta-carotene productivity is around 0.05 g/L per day 
(Borowitzka 2010; Bogacz-Radomska and Harasym 2018). To keep up with the increasing demand new pro-
duction hosts are needed. In recent years cell factories have been based on Escherichia coli, Saccharomyces 
cerevisiae and the non-conventional oleaginous yeast Yarrowia lipolytica. The highest titer yet, was reported 
in 2017 for a recombinant Y. lipolytica  strain producing 6.5 g/L of beta-carotene (Larroude et al. 2017). 
 
1.1. Aim  
 
The overall aim of this Master’s thesis is to optimise the production of beta-carotene, produced by the re-
combinant strain Yarrowia lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, through media optimisation.  
The base-line, which should be optimised upon, is mainly based on preliminary results by Larroude et al. 
(2017) who constructed the strain (Larroude et al. 2017) and cultivations in minimal medium applying stand-
ard conditions for Yarrowia (this study). In the study “A synthetic biology approach to transform Yarrowia 
lipolytica into a competitive biotechnological producer of β-carotene” a single fed-batch experiment, in rich 
YPD medium, yielded a total beta-carotene titer of 6.5 g/L a yield of 0.090 g beta-carotene/g dry weight. For 
the initial batch fermentations (this study), in minimal medium, the titer was 0,364 g/L and the productivity 
was 0.053 g beta-carotene/g dry weight.  
Optimization will be carried out mainly in micro-fermentations, and fermentations in 1L scale with minimal 
medium. Some experiments will be carried out using complex media components to potentially further in-
crease the production of beta-carotene and to simulate an industrial process.  
The main focus of the optimisation strategies will be to improve the production of the precursor metabolite 
acetyl-CoA, as it is used in the biosynthesis of beta-carotene in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF through the meva-
lonate pathway. Larroude et al. (2017) linked the beta-carotene pathway to lipogenesis, as they both have 
acetyl-CoA as precursor (Larroude et al. 2017).  
Due to the novelty of the strain, its general characteristics have not yet been described. Another partial aim 
is therefore to characterise the strain in terms of morphology, substrate consumption, growth pattern, 
growth rate, yields etc. and compare it to its parental strain, the lipid overproducer, Y. lipolytica Y3820.

1.2. Background  
 
1.2.1. Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF by Larroude et al.  
Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF was the result of the bioinformatical and molecular biology work carried out by 
Larroude et al. in 2017. The aim was to construct a recombinant cell factory for beta-carotene production at 
an industrially relevant scale.  
 
The oleaginous nature of the genus Yarrowia leads to a high natural production of acetyl-CoA. Acetyl CoA is 
a precursor for both the mevalonate pathway (leading to beta-carotene) and the lipogenesis. Yarrowia was, 
therefore, chosen for heterologous production of beta-carotene. The parental strain was Y. lipolytica  
Y38201, a recombinant strain that had been engineered to overproduce and accumulate lipids. Y. lipolytica 
Y3820 was derived from the wild type strain W29.  A lipid overproducing strain was chosen as the scaffold 
due to its high lipid production and thereby large pool of acetyl-CoA (Larroude et al. 2017). Another motiva-
tion for choosing Y. lipolytica was, that its genome contains a large part of the biosynthetic pathway for ter-
penoid synthesis. The yeast already synthesises geranyl-geranyl pyrophosphate, which is three biosynthetic 
steps from beta-carotene. Figure 1 shows the engineered pathways of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. It can 
noted that the three heterogeneous enzymatic steps (shown in red), are performed by two enzymes, as 
CarRP is bi-functional. Figure 1 also shows that glucose is metabolized to acetyl-CoA in the glycolysis, which 
is then utilised as a building block in the lipogenesis. Triacylglycerides (TAGs) can be mobilised to free fatty 
acids (FFA), which can be beta-oxidised to acetyl-CoA. After the beta-oxidation acetyl-CoA will be shuttled 
back to the free pool, from where it can enter the mevalonate pathway. The genes responsible for the mobi-
lisation and beta-oxidation are TGL4 and POX1-6. According to Figure 2 those genes were deleted in the con-
struction of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. It has previously been reported that overexpression of hy-
droxymethylglutaryl-CoA reductase (HMG1) is associated with a higher flux towards carotenoid production, 
as the reaction it catalyses is rate limiting in the mevalonate pathway (Larroude et al. 2017).

β-carotene”. Original legend: “Scheme of the metabolic pathways leading to the production of lipids and β-carotene in Y. lipolytica . 
Metabolites are shown in black, native enzymes in purple and heterologous enzymes in red. The continuous arrows indicate a meta-

In the experimental work, described by the authors, Golden Gate assembly was used  to carry out promotor 
shuffling and to pair promotors to genes, for the purpose of finding optimal combinations. Plasmids contain-
ing the heterologous genes in a gene cassette (called the car-casette), were constructed using Golden Gate 
assembly. The cassette was able to contain three genes under three promotors. A table of the genes and 
promotors used can be seen in Table 1.

The promoter shuffling proved that the Translational Elongation factor 1 (TEF) promotor achieved the high-
est expression of the genes. The products of the Golden Gate reactions were transformed into competent E. 
coli cells, digested and transformed into Y. lipolytica. The identification of promotors at each specific site was 
performed by Polymerase Chain Reaction (PCR). The final strain was constructed as illustrated in Figure 2. 
Promotor shuffling was carried out in the in the wild type (left-hand side of Figure 2).  A number of genetic 
modifications (pTEF-DGA2, pTEF-PDP1, Dpox1-6, Dtgl4) were conducted in the wildtype to obtain the lipid 
overproducer, Y3820, denoted “ob” (for obese). One car-cassette was inserted, followed by the HMG1 cas-
sette, also under the TEF1 promotor, and two subsequent car-cassettes. The result was a lipid overproducing 
strain that contained three gene cassettes for beta-carotene production and one cassette that increases the 
flux towards carotenoid production.

From the fermentation broth, biomass concentration and metabolite concentrations were analysed. The re-
sults were a total production of 6.5 g/L of beta-carotene and a yield of beta-carotene in reference to sub-
strate of 0.090 mg beta-carotene/g dry weight along with a total lipid production of 42.6 g/L. The authors 
investigated the localization of the beta-carotene product. The found that all the hydrophobic beta-carotene 
was stored intracellularly in lipid bodies along with storage lipids. They developed an extraction protocol for 
lab scale experiments and beta-carotene quantification  (Larroude et al. 2017).  
 
1.2.2.  Yarrowia lipolytica   
Yarrowia lipolytica is a non-conventional yeast as opposed to the conventional yeasts like S. cerevisiae and 
Schizosaccharomyces pombe. Yeasts are unicellular eukaryotes which all belong to the kingdom of Fungi. 
Furthermore, they are divided between the phyla of Ascomycota and Basidomycota, where Yarrowia be-
longs in the first. Yarrowia fits into the class Saccharomytes, where Saccharomyces and Pichia also belong 
(Darvishi Harzevili 2014).  
The relation of Y. lipolytica to a few other organisms, within the Kingdom Fungi, can be seen in Figure 3. Ba-
sidomycota is exemplified by Agaricus bisporus (common mushroom).  
 
==PAGE:15==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 15 of 154 
 
 
Figure 3: Phylogenetic representation of the relationship of Yarrowia lipolytica to other organisms within the kingdom Fungi.   
The figure has been designed using various tools. Firstly, organisms were organized using NCBI Common Tree 
(https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Taxonomy/CommonTree/wwwcmt.cgi) and then visualized using CLC Main Workbench (version 7.7.3, 
QIAGEN Aarhus).  
The presented distances do not resemble relational distance.  
The general morphology of Yarrowia varies based on a range of parameters, including; genetics, overall 
growth conditions, carbon source, nitrogen source, aeration, agitation, temperature and pH. Yarrowia cells 
are ellipsoid, the main component in the cell walls is galactomannans, which are polysaccharides composed 
of a mannose backbone with galactose as the side-groups. Single cells can elongate into true hyphae and 
pseudohyphae can be formed, see Figure 4.  
 
 
 
Figure 4: The dimorphic nature of Yarrowia visualised. Left panel: single cells from exponentially growing liquid culture. Right panel: 
filamentous cells from a plate that had been stored at 5° C.  
Pictures from this study (Jacobsen 2018) 
 
The hyphae are formed because Y.  lipolytica is dimorphic, which means that it can switch between two mor-
phologies. The transition into true hyphal growth is dependent on the medium and the growth phase the 
organism is currently in. Media components like glucose, oils, ammonium and nitrogen-containing extracts 
especially stimulate hyphae formation. Others inhibit hyphae formation, those include; the presence of cys-
teines or the absence of magnesium sulphate or ion chloride. Non-biological factors also affect the morphol-
ogy of Yarrowia; in a simple batch-mode acidic (pH 4.5 and below) and neutral/alkaline (pH 7 and up) over 
84% of the yeast population favours a filamentous morphology. In a chemostat cultivation the yeast grows 
as single cells. The exact reason has not been determined but some include; growth kinetics are different as 
==PAGE:16==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 16 of 154 
 
the batch has dynamic kinetics, whereas the chemostat has constant kinetics. The age of the cells is also dif-
ferent in the two cultivation types. The mycelium consists of septate hyphae that each have a length of a 
couple millimetres and a width of 3-5 µm. The septa resemble Ascomycete central spores, which is uncom-
mon to most filamentous yeasts. Segments are around 50-70 µm and contain one nucleus.  
On the surfaces the colony morphology can range from smooth to heavily convoluted, and from glistening to 
mat (Darvishi Harzevili 2014; Timoumi et al. 2017).  
 
Sexual and asexual reproduction has been reported in Y. lipolytica. In asexual reproduction, the buds form 
from any place on the mother cell (multilateral budding). Most Y. lipolytica isolates characterised have been 
haploid, strongly indicating that the yeast has a stable haploid life cycle. In sexual reproduction asci are 
formed and attached to hyphae. Each ascus contains one to four ascospores. The formation of ascospores 
can be induced by cultivation in yeast extract or malt extract medium. In general, the mating frequency is 
very low (Darvishi Harzevili 2014). 
 
The reference strain CLIB122 of Y. lipolytica has a genome of 20.5 mega bases and the content of gua-
nine/cytosine base pairs is 49%. The 7357 genes are found on six chromosomes. Y.  lipolytica is suitable for 
genetic engineering as creating mutants is a simple process. The consensus sequences of the promotors are 
similar to those of S. cerevisiae and in general efficient protocols have been developed for transformation, 
expression and secretion of heterologous gene products (Darvishi Harzevili 2014). 
 
Y.  lipolytica is strictly aerobic and is therefore sensitive to oxygen limitation. The majority of the adenosine 
triphosphate (ATP) produced in the cell is from the oxidative phosphorylation.Y.  lipolytica can utilize a wide 
range of compounds as carbon source, these include; glucose, N-acetyl-D-glucosamine, FA, TAG and sugar 
alcohols (Darvishi Harzevili 2014). The performance on glucose and glycerol in submerged cultivations was 
investigated by Workman et al. in 2013 for the wildtype W29 strain. The authors found that the specific 
growth rate was higher when utilizing glycerol (µ = 0.3 h-1) than for glucose utilisaton (µ = 0.24 h-1). Glycerol 
was preferred in a co-substrate fermentation. The reasons given by the authors were that the genome of Y.  
lipolytica encode only one hexose transporter but three proteins involved in glycerol transport. Further-
more, glycerol can enter the metabolism by two different routes (either with docosahexaenioc acid or glyc-
erol-3-phosphate as intermediate) after it has passed the cell membrane, as visualised in Figure 5.  
 
==PAGE:17==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 17 of 154 
 
 
Figure 5: Simplified overview of main routes of glucose and glycerol uptake and metabolism. The arrows represent one or multiple 
enzymatic steps.  
The figure has been inspired by (Workman, Holt, and Thykaer 2013) and (Seraphim Papanikolaou et al. 2013). The figure was con-
structed using ChemDraw version 16.0.1.4 to generate the structural formulas and the biological structures. 
It was determined that the pathway using glycerol-3-phosphate is the primary one, the other pathway is also 
active. It can also be noted that Y. lipolytica synthesizes a variety of precursors which are used in the biosyn-
thesis of small organic compounds that have industrial relevance. The polyols erythritol, mannitol and arabi-
tol are native to Yarrowia. The polyols are shown in Figure 5 (Workman, Holt, and Thykaer 2013).  
 
Besides sugars and sugar alcohols Y. lipolytica has the potential to use hydrophobic compounds, such as al-
kanes, FAs and TAGs, as carbon source. A simplified overview of these substrates, their pathways and subcel-
lular location can be seen in Figure 6.  
==PAGE:18==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 18 of 154 
 
 
Figure 6: Simplified visual overview of hydrophobic substrates in Yarrowia. The pathway and localization of the substrates are shown. 
Arrows represent one or more enzymatic steps.  
The figure was constructed in ChemDraw version 16.0.1.4 and based on Figure 5 in (Darvishi Harzevili 2014).  
 
The hydrophobic substrates, outlined in Figure 6, are degraded in the cell. Absorption and transport into the 
cell are common to all of them. Unknown uptake systems facilitate the passage over the cell membrane. Af-
ter being taken up the substrates are transported to their location of primary oxidation. 
The three hydrophobic substrates enter the metabolism by one of two distinct pathways. TAGs can either be 
stored directly in lipid bodies (LB) (shown in yellow) or be degraded extracellularly, by enzymes, to FA. The 
alkanes and FA follow pathways, that are very similar to each other; in the endoplasmic reticulum (shown in 
red) they are degraded either to fatty alcohols, FAs or fatty-acyl-CoA. All three degradation products can en-
ter the peroxisome (shown in green) and fatty acids can directly enter the LBs where they are stored as stor-
age lipids. In the peroxisome the fatty alcohols, fatty acids and fatty-acyl CoA are converted into acetyl-CoA 
or propionyl-CoA (in the case of an odd numbered alkane) by beta-oxidation. Under some environmental 
conditions the fatty acids (localised in the peroxisome) can also be activated and stored in the lipid bodies or 
incorporated into phospholipids. Acetyl-CoA and propionyl-CoA from the beta-oxidation can enter either the 
glyoxylate cycle in the peroxisome, which is linked to the tri-carboxylic acid cycle (TCA) or the citrate cycle in 
the mitochondrion (shown in blue). Acetyl-CoA and propionyl-CoA can also be exchanged between the gly-
oxylate cycle and the citrate cycle.  The genus of Yarrowia does also hold the potential to produce emulsifi-
ers, that can facilitate the conversion of the substrates into micelles that are more easily absorbed by the 
cell (Darvishi Harzevili 2014).  
 
The lipid pathway and lipid synthesis of Y. lipolytica is particularly interesting. The lipids can be synthesised 
from glucose, glycerol or from elongation of acetyl-CoA or propionyl-CoA as outlined in Figure 7. The lipids 
can also enter the metabolism directly as outlined in Figure 6. Yarrowia is known to be able to accumulate 
large amounts of lipids within the cells. The highest reported values are over 50% lipids in the cell dry 
weight. The accumulated lipids are TAGs of chain lengths of C:16, C:18 and higher along with steryl esters 
==PAGE:19==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 19 of 154 
 
(SE) and in some cases FFA. Those lipids form LBs in the cytoplasm, the core of which are extremely hydro-
phobic. The main function of the LB is to store energy in the form of neutral lipids for mobilization under cer-
tain conditions. In recent years it has been concluded that LB also play a role in the synthesis and degrada-
tion of TAGs. Highly hydrophobic heterologous products produced by the organism will also reside in the li-
pid bodies.   
 
To synthesise TAGs acyl-CoA and Glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate (G3P) are needed. The G3P can be delivered 
from two different pathways; either from glycerol, which is taken up as a substrate by the cell, or from DHAP 
phosphate synthesised from glucose. To yield TAG the G3P needs to have three acyl groups added. The first 
two are added, through three enzymatic steps, from acetyl-CoA or propionyl CoA to yield a diglyceride 
(DAG); shown in the transition between the grey and reddish-orange section. The last acyl group can be 
added by two distinct pathways; directly from acyl-CoA using an acetyl-CoA depended pathway or from a 
glycerophospholipid using an acetyl-CoA independent pathway. The synthesised TAGs are stored in the LBs. 
The two pathways, along with the further fate of the synthesised TAGs, are outlined in Figure 7.  
 
 
Figure 7: Visual overview of the main pathways that are involved in the synthesis and utilization of storage lipids in Y. lipolytica. The 
figure is created using elements from ChemDraw version 16.0.1.4 and based on Figure 1 in (Beopoulos et al. 2008).  
The stored lipids can be mobilised when the organism needs the stored energy. Three metabolic states lead 
to the mobilisation of lipids; during the exponential growth phase when the lipids are needed in the synthe-
sis of membrane lipids to maintain rapid growth and division, during the stationary phase when nutrients are 
depleted, or to exit from a stage of starvation, for instance when nutrients are once again available.  
To degrade TAGs they firstly need to be mobilised. These reactions are carried out by the triacylglycerol li-
pase (TGL) enzymes. In the mobilisation the fatty acid chains are separated from the glycerol. After the mo-
bilisation the actual degradation can begin. The FFAs produced in the mobilisation phase are degraded by a 
beta-oxidation pathway to yield acetyl-CoA units. The POX 1-6 enzymes are acyl-CoA oxidases, they are lo-
cated in the peroxisomes and they are key enzymes in the beta-oxidation as they can control the size of the 
TAG pool. Other enzymes aid the degradation to acetyl-CoA after the POX enzymes (Beopoulos et al. 2008).

Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 20 of 154 
 
 
1.2.3. Carotenoids  
 
Carotenoids are a group of C40 polyenes mainly produced by plants as they are crucial in photosynthesis.  
Examples of carotenoids are: lycopene, beta-carotene and astaxanthin. The structures of these three com-
pounds can be seen in Figure 8. The differences in the structures of the molecules are towards the ends, the 
middle is conserved.  
 
 
 
 
Figure 8: Examples of carotenoids.  
Created in ChemDraw version 16.0.1.4 based on (Dewick 2009b).  
 
The exact colour of the carotenoid compound is determined by the number of conjugated double bonds in 
the C40 chain. The intensity if the colour increases with the increasing number of conjugated double bonds. 
In plants, the red and yellow colours of carotenoids aid the harvest of light in the blue spectrum, the energy 
can then be transferred onto chlorophyll in the cell. The maintenance of the balance between absorbing 
light and avoiding oxidative damage from overexposure to light. is carried out by carotenoids. A group of ca-
rotenoids called xanthophylls facilitates removal of excess light energy by non-photochemical quenching. 
The chlorophyll that contains the excess energy is in the exited state, to return to the ground state the xan-
tophylls aid the conversion of the photons to heat. Triplet chlorophylls are in a harmful excited state. They 
will react with oxygen and create ROS that can harm the cell. Carotenoids can quench the triplets by energy 
transfer in a Dexter reaction. The reaction occurs when the triplet and the carotenoid are in close proximity. 
Carotenoids have antioxidant properties, accumulation of those will aid the maintenance of cellular homeo-
stasis (Cuttriss et al. 2011;Ballottari et al. 2013).

Beta-carotene
O
OH
O

1.2.3.1 Biosynthesis of carotenoids  
Carotenoids are terpenes with a length of 40 carbon atoms, this specific class is called tetraterpenes. Ter-
penes are secondary metabolites along with alkaloids, shikimic acid derived compounds and polyketides; a 
general visualisation of some of the primary metabolism and the secondary metabolism can be seen in Fig-
ure 9 (Dewick 2009a; Dewick 2009b).  
 
 
Figure 9: Simplified overview of some of the primary metabolism and selected pathways of the secondary metabolism. The glycolysis 
is visualized by dark blue arrows. The shikimic acid pathway is visualized by purple arrows. The polyketide pathway is visualized by 
yellow-ish beige arrows. The pathway to alkaloids is shown by light blue arrows. The terpenoid pathway is divided into two pathways; 
the mevalonic aid dependent pathway (green) and the mevalonic independent pathway (red). The orange area signifies the steps 
common to both pathways.  
Structures are created in in ChemDraw version 16.0.1.4 based on (Dewick 2009b) and inspired by Professor Thomas Ostenfeld Larsen 
(DTU Bioengineering).  
As can be seen in Figure 9 the secondary metabolites are based on products from the primary metabolism. 
The primary metabolism is visualised by the glycolysis and the TCA cycle (dark and light blue arrows). Pri-
mary metabolites are produced continuously by the cell and they are essential to the cell. The secondary 
metabolism is exemplified by the following pathways; the shikimic acid pathway which produces aromatic 
amino acids, which are used in the synthesis of alkaloids, enzymes and proteins, the malonic acid/polyketide 
pathway where polyketides are synthesised and lastly; the terpenoid pathway. The terpenoid pathway is di-
vided into two parts; the mevalonic acid dependent pathway which has 3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl-CoA 
==PAGE:22==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 22 of 154 
 
(HMG-CoA) and mevalonic acid as the intermediates and the mevalonic acid independent pathway which 
converts acetyl-CoA directly to Isopentenyl pyrophosphate (IPP). The two pathways join at IPP to yield terpe-
noids, where the carotenoids are a subgroup. Many secondary metabolites do not strictly belong in one of 
the described categories; rather they are combinations of two or more. The secondary metabolites are not 
essential to normal growth of the cell. Their exact purpose is still not fully elucidated, but it is hypothesised 
that they enhance the chances of survival for the cell, through antimicrobial activity, toxicity or the potential 
to attract other organisms of their species. Secondary metabolite profiles are based on many parameters, 
including; genus and species of the organism, conditions, presence of other organisms and stress (Croteau, 
Kutchan, and Lewis 2000; Dewick 2009a; O’Connor 2015).  
 
The class of terpenoids is of special interest as carotenoids belong to it. The building blocks of terpeniods are 
isoprene units, which are five carbon species, the structural formula can be seen in Figure 10. The isoprene 
units have a head region and a tail region. When isoprene units are linked it is usually in a “head-to-tail” 
manner.  
 
 
Figure 10: An isoprene unit. The five carbon species has a head region (branched) and a tail region (straight).  
Created in ChemDraw version 16.0.1.4 based on (Dewick 2009b). 
As shown in Figure 9, mevalonic acid is composed of acetyl-CoA units; three molecules are needed in order 
to yield one mevalonic acid molecule. The first two molecules combine in a Claisen condensation (a conden-
sation where a good leaving group is present, in this case one of the thiols with CoA attached) and the last 
unit is added by an aldol addition (a condensation reaction where a good leaving group is not present, there-
fore an alcohol is lost) yielding the intermediate HMG-CoA, which is irreversibly converted to mevalonic acid 
in a two-step reduction of the thiol group to a primary alcohol. This conversion of HMG-CoA to mevalonic 
acid by HMG-CoA reductase, is the rate limiting step. IPP is formed from mevalonic acid which is phosphory-
lated and dehydrated by ATP-dependent enzymes. Isomerase enzymes facilitate the isomerisation of IPP into 
dimethylallyl pyrophosphate (DMAPP) where a proton is lost, which is also shown in Figure 9. IPP and 
DMAPP are not only isomers of each other, they are also reactive hemiterpene intermediates in the terpe-
noid pathway as shown in Figure 11. Figure 11 also illustrates the pathway from simple C5 terpenoids to 
complex C40 terpenoids. DMAPP and IPP are combined in an enzymatic reaction that involves the ionisation 
of IPP and the loss of the proton, a reaction that is very similar to the one occurring in the isomerisation of 
IPP to DMAPP. The product is geranyl pyrophosphate (GPP), which is a monoterpenoid. The addition of an-
other C5 IPP unit to GPP yields a sesquiterpene (exemplified by farnesyl pyrophosphate (FPP) in Figure 11). 
Sesquiterpenes are usually synthesised in the mevalonate pathway, rather than in the MEP pathway. FPP is 
an important precursor, which can be converted into both linear, mono-, bi and tricyclic sesquiterpene com-
pounds. Following the same pattern as described for sesquiterpenes, the C20 diterpenes are synthesised by 
addition of yet another C5 IPP unit to FPP.  The result of the addition is geranylgeranyl pyrophosphate 
(GGPP) that gives rise to all diterpenes. The exact same pattern is seen for the C25 terpenes, called sesterter-
penes; IPP is added onto GGPP to yield geranylfarnesyl pyrophosphate. For triterpenes, which are C30 com-
pounds, the pattern no longer applies. Rather than adding on yet another IPP to the growing chain, the 
triterpenes are synthesised from two molecules of FPP, which are joined in a tail-to-tail manner to form 
Isoprene
==PAGE:23==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 23 of 154 
 
squalene. The resemblance of squalene to FPP and the symmetry of the molecule is evident in Figure 11. The 
mode of reaction that leads to the C40 tetrapeptides is nearly identical to the one that formed triterpenes; 
two molecules of GGPP are combined, tail-to-tail, to form phytoene that is the skeleton of all tetraterpenes. 
Carotenoids is the only group of compounds which are C40 terpenes (Dewick 2009b).   
 
Figure 11: Overview of the terpenoid pathway from C5 units to C40 tetraterpeniods.   
The figure is adapted from Figure 5.3 in Medicinal Natural Products: A Biosynthetic Approach, 3rd Edition,  Paul M. Dewick (Dewick 
2009b) 
1.2.3.2. Beta-carotene; properties, producers and potential 
Beta-carotene is a tetraterpenoid, which is synthesised from the linear precursor phytoene in a cyclisation 
event. The structure of beta-carotene is shown in both Figure 8 and Figure 11. As mentioned earlier the ca-
rotenoids share the middle region and therefore the ring structures, at the ends, is what characterises beta-
carotene. The colour of beta-carotene is mostly known from carrots, where the orange colour is predomi-
nant. Beta-carotene is present in most higher plants (Dewick 2009b). In its pure form the compound is red 
(Ribeiro, Barreto, and Coelho 2011).  
 
The applications of beta-carotene are diverse and numerous. Like all carotenoids, beta-carotene has antioxi-
dant properties due to the binding capacity, of the conjugated double bonds, for singlet oxygen. ROS are 
==PAGE:24==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 24 of 154 
 
therefore eliminated. The antioxidant properties are exploited for sun protective purposes; when taken 
orally it can prevent human skin from sunburns and photoaging because the blood plasma levels of beta-
carotene will increase. The desired effect is only achieved if beta-carotene supplements have been con-
sumed for weeks. In the food industry beta-carotene is utilised as a natural colouring agent to achieve yel-
low and orange colours. Beta-carotene is also known as pro-vitamin A (along with other cyclic carotenoids) 
that can be converted into mature vitamin A in the human body. The most important function of vitamin A is 
to maintain eye sight, as the chromophores of the pigments in the eye are composed of vitamin A (retinal). 
Due to this function beta-carotene also prevents night blindness and blindness in general. Furthermore vita-
min A aids to maintain growth and reproductivity, it has also been used in the prevention and elimination of 
acne (Ribeiro, Barreto, and Coelho 2011).  
 
Besides having applications of its own, beta-carotene can also be enzymatically converted into other indus-
trially relevant compounds. One is zeaxanthin; a carotenoid which requires one enzymatic step in the con-
version from beta-carotene. Zeaxanthin is one of the predominant carotenoids of the macular pigment, 
which is the yellow spot near the centre of the retina, in human and animal eyes. These carotenoids prevent 
light-induced damage to the retina and degradation of the macular. Lutein is an isomer of zeaxanthin, which 
is related to cognitive health and is the predominant carotenoid in infant and aging brains. The relation be-
tween zeaxanthin and cognitive health and function has not yet been fully elucidated (Johnson 2014; 
Landrum and Bone 2001). Astaxanthin is another carotenoid which can be synthesized from beta-carotene 
through two additional enzymatic steps. Astaxanthin has a red colour, it is the pigment which is utilised for 
improving the colour of farmed fish and eggs. Recently the interest for using astaxanthin in more food prod-
ucts and cosmetics has increased. A study from The Novo Nordisk Center for Biosustainability at the Tech-
nical University of Denmark constructed a recombinant Y. lipolytica strain which could express the genes re-
sponsible for astaxanthin (Kildegaard et al. 2017).  
  
Beta-carotene is produced naturally in a wide range of organisms including microalgae, filamentous fungi, 
yeast and bacteria; this natural production is exploited for commercial production in the biotechnological 
industry. The main industrial producer is the algae D. salina. Its beta-carotene productivity is around 0.05 g/L 
per day. Cell factories based on organisms including:  Phycomyces sp., S. cerevisiae, Y. lipolytica and Halomo-
nas elongate have also been constructed, with heterologous genes from various sources, for industrial pro-
duction. Chemical extraction from vegetables is yet another way to produce beta-carotene from an organic 
source. As of 2011 only 2% of the total beta-carotene production originated from the organic sources de-
scribed here. The remaining 98% originated from chemical synthesis, which is based on the precursor beta-
ionone, the structure of which can be seen in Figure 12. At first beta-ionone was derived from natural 
sources but currently it chemically synthesized from acetone or butadiene. Product yields from the process 
range between 60% and 85% (Ribeiro, Barreto, and Coelho 2011). 
 
Figure 12: Structural formula of beta-ionene that is the precursor in chemical synthesis of beta-carotene.

The market size for beta-carotene was 432.2 million American dollars in 2015 and is estimated to continue 
increasing to around 600 million American dollars in 2024 (Grand View Resarch 2016). The total market for 
carotenoids is expected to reach 1.4 billion American dollars in 2018 (Larroude et al. 2017).  
 
1.2.3.3. Quantification of beta-carotene 
Beta-carotene can be identified and quantified by photometric methods; the conjugated double bonds facili-
tate the ability to absorb light with optima at 453 and 480 nm (Craft and Soares 1992). Any analytical 
method that can detect an absorption at 450 nm is suitable for beta-carotene, some examples will be given 
here:  
 
Classical spectrophotometry:  
Larroude et al. quantified the production of beta-carotene by Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF by classical visual 
light spectrophotometry and creation of a standard curve from reference material (Larroude et al. 2017).  
 
High Performance Liquid Chromatography-based method:  
Chromatography is based on the same principles as simple extraction; the analyte will interact with the 
phase it is most chemically similar to. HPLC exploits this principle; it is based on a two-phase system. One 
phase is the mobile phase; it carries the analytes through the system. The other is the stationary phase, 
which is a column packed with beads, with has a specific chemical property. The analyte will then interact 
more with the phase than the other, which leads to a separation.There are two main configurations for 
HPLC. The first, and most commonly used, is reversed phase HPLC (RP-HPLC) where the column is the non-
polar component; C18 columns (silica beads with C18 chains) are commonly used, the polar component is 
the mobile phase. RP-HPLC can be used for many types of analytes. The other configuration is called normal 
phase HPLC (NP-HPLC) and it is the opposite of RP-HPLC: the column is polar and the mobile phase is non-
polar. When the analytes have been separated based on their polarity, they need to be detected and quanti-
fied; a simple detector is the photodiode array detector (DAD) which is a spectrophotometric method, like 
the classical spectrophotometric method described above. The DAD is a flow cell and the detector records 
the entire spectrum as the analyte is eluted. The analytes can be identified based on known libraries and 
quantified based on a standard curve constructed from reference material (Harris 2016).  
 
A research team from the Novo Nordisk Center for Biosustainability at the Technical University of Denmark 
developed a RP-HPLC based method with a DAD detector for the quantification of carotenoids produced in 
Y. lipolytica. The method used a C18 column and reference material for standard curves (Kildegaard et al. 
2017). Other HPLC-DAD based methods are also known. The company Phenomenex, which manufacturers 
chromatography columns, has developed a method based on a C8 column (Phenomenex 2018).  
 
The solubility of beta-carotene in solvents appropriate for analysis is poor. For instance; in ethanol the solu-
bility is only 30 mg/L (Craft and Soares 1992).  
  
1.2.4. Fermentation technology and kinetics  
Fermentation, as a term, is very broad and have many interpretations. The strict biochemical interpretation 
is associated to the catabolism of organic compounds in order to harvest energy. In an industrial and bio-
technological context the interpretation is broader and more inclusive. It is related to the conversion of a 
substrate into a product by a living organism mainly in a submerged environment. The range of products 
==PAGE:26==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 26 of 154 
 
produced commercially is very diverse and covers, among others, biomass, enzymes, recombinant proteins, 
natural products etc. (Stanbury, Whitaker, and Hall 2017a).  
 
Fermentation technology and the associated methods are also used extensively in academia for physiologi-
cal characterisation and screening of new organisms or potential cell factories (Knudsen 2015). Fermentation 
can aid metabolic engineering research, especially through metabolic flux analysis (MFA). The method uses 
labelled substrates to map and quantify both the catabolic and anabolic fluxes within the central metabolism 
of the living cell. The information can be used to elucidate pathways and gain knowledge on the best targets 
for engineering (Wiechert 2001).  
 
1.2.4.1. Fermentation media 
Fermentation processes are carried out in closed environments, therefore the medium needs to contain all 
nutrients needed for the organism to sustain its own metabolism. The optimal medium is based on some 
variables: organism, desired product, process and the aim of the cultivation. Some media components are 
universal to all media; water, energy source, carbon source, nitrogen source, minerals and metals. Vitamins 
and oxygen are also required by a large portion of processes (Stanbury, Whitaker, and Hall 2017a).  
 
Two types of media exist; they both fulfil the requirements of the cell. A defined or synthetic medium is 
based on pure compounds where the precise composition is known. A defined medium can be designed to 
meet all the requirements from the cell or to yield a very specific product. Defined media are often used in 
research, as they have a high degree of reproducibility and allow for complex calculations. Furthermore, the 
medium is clear, has a low tendency for foaming, is easy to sterilise and allows for easy downstream pro-
cessing. The other type of medium is called a complex medium or natural medium. The components are not 
chemically defined, but rather industrially produced raw materials like molasses, corn steep liquor and yeast 
extract. As the medium is complex it contains a high number of compounds that the cell needs and therefore 
do not have to synthesise de novo. On the other hand some compounds might be present in limiting 
amounts, and it is therefore necessary to combine media component to overcome this. Foaming can be an 
issue when using complex media formulations. The price of complex media is much lower than that of de-
fined media, therefore they are very popular in industrial production processes (Kampen 2014).      
 
1.2.4.1.1. Media components and requirements  
The minimal requirement of a medium should be based on the elemental composition of the cell in question 
and thereby indirectly on its metabolism. Table 4 shows the elemental composition of three subgroups of 
microorganisms these are: bacteria, yeast and filamentous fungi.  The numbers in Table 4 are given as per-
centages in dry biomass. The numbers are standard values and do therefore not take genetically modified 
organisms into account.

Along with the compounds mentioned in Table 4 some additional components might be required. Metals 
besides iron, can be required for enzymatic activity. Some organisms do not have the machinery to perform 
de novo synthesis of some amino acids, vitamins or nucleotides. These deficiencies can either be characteris-
tics of the organism or results of genetic engineering (Stanbury, Whitaker, and Hall 2017a).  
 
1.2.4.1.2.Yeast metabolism and its “Bow-Tie” structure 
To design the optimal growth medium for a specific organism, it is advantageous to know and consider its 
metabolism.  The metabolism of any organism is the sum of its catabolic and anabolic reactions. Over many 
years of evolution the metabolism has become highly conserved and evolved, which has resulted in the 
“Bow-Tie Structure of Metabolism”, of which a visualisation can be seen in Figure 13. The nutrients are trans-
ported into the cell and broken down in catabolic reactions. The catabolic process results in 12 precursor 
metabolites, these are; glucose-6-phosphate, fructose-6-phosphate, ribose-5-phosphate, erythrose-4-phos-
phate, glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate, 3-phosphoglycerate, phosphoenol-pyruvate, pyruvate, acetyl-CoA, 2-
oxoglutarate, succinyl-CoA, and oxaloacetate. The precursors are combined with the products of other cata-
bolic reaction or biosynthetic reactions to form the building blocks; sugars, amino acids, nucleotides, fatty 
acids and co-factors, which are shown in red (Nielsen and Keasling 2016).   
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:28==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 28 of 154 
 
 
Figure 13: The Bow-Tie structure of metabolism. Nutrients are transported into the cell, where they are broken down into the 12 core 
precursor metabolites. The precursors then participate in anabolic processes along with other nutrients to make up building blocks 
which fall into the categories: sugars, amino acids, nucleotides, fatty acids and co-factors. Through polymerization reactions larger 
molecules like proteins, genes and cellular components are produced. A more detailed visualization of the reactions and pathways 
can be seen in Figure 14.  
Figure was inspired by Professor MSO Mikael Rørdam Andersen and freely adapted from (Nielsen and Keasling 2016) 
All of the above-mentioned precursor metabolites contain carbon. They are degradation products in the cen-
tral carbon metabolism, which is simplified and outlined in Figure 14. In yeast the central carbon metabolism 
is composed of three pathways. The glycolysis (Embden-Meyerhof pathway) is the main entry point for sug-
ars into the central carbon metabolism. Through numerous reaction in the glycolysis glucose is converted 
into pyruvate, and energy is generated in the process. The glycolysis is responsible for six of the twelve pre-
cursor metabolites. Polysaccharides, lipids and amino acids can all be synthesised from the precursors from 
the glycolysis. The polysaccharides can be polymerised to form cell wall. The amino acids are polymerised to 
proteins. The TCA cycle (Krebs cycle) converts pyruvate into acetyl-CoA in its first step. The TCA cycle pro-
vides the cell with building blocks, four of which are the mentioned precursors. Co-factors are also produced 
in the TCA cycle. Building blocks are produced under both anaerobic and aerobic conditions. Organisms with 
the ability to respire can also synthesise energy in the TCA cycle.  The pentose-phosphate pathway (PP path-
way) is a multi-purpose pathway in the central carbon metabolism. The last two of the twelve pre-cursor 
metabolites are synthesised here. One of which is ribose-5-phosphate, which is essential in the synthesis of 
genetic material. The co-factor Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate (NADPH) is synthesised in the 
PP pathway, it is responsible for delivering reducing power anabolic reactions (Nielsen and Keasling 2016; 
Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 2011)

Irene Hjorth Jacobsen

Page 29 of 154 
 
  
Figure 14: The overall core metabolism, in this case based around the central carbon metabolism. The 12 precursor metabolites are 
highlighted. From each metabolite the products they participate in are shown. Monomeric building blocks can polymerize into poly-
mers; proteins, polysaccharides, DNA, RNA and lipids.

1.2.4.1.3. Media components 
 
Water  
Water is the first requirement for life as we know it, as all components within cells are suspended in the cy-
toplasm, which is a watery solution.  
On the water activity scale microbial growth is seen between 0.60 and 0.97, below 0.60 no proliferation is 
seen, but microbial structures can lay dormant (Fontana and Campbell 2008). 
 
Gibbs free energy  
Gibbs free energy, in the form of ATP is used for running the metabolism of the cell. Cells can harvest energy 
for growth using different mechanisms, based on their metabolism. The most common methods are oxida-
tion of media components, and conversion of light energy into chemical energy. Chemoorganotrophic organ-
isms utilize the carbon source as energy source. ATP is generated in the glycolysis under all conditions. The 
largest amount of ATP can be generated in the oxidative phosphorylation, a process that requires oxygen as 
the final electron acceptor. ATP can also be synthesised from pyruvate through fermentation under anaero-
bic growth conditions. (Stanbury, Whitaker, and Hall 2017b; Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 2011). In 1960, 
Bauchop and Elsden proved that the biomass produced by an organism is proportional to the energy source 
provided to it from the medium. They proved this statement by cultivation of both prokaryotic and eukary-
otic organisms (Bauchop and Elsden 1960). 
 
Carbon  
It is estimated that approximately 50 percent of dry biomass is carbon. Carbon is included in all organic cellu-
lar components; cell wall, proteins, genetic material, cell membrane, primary and secondary metabolites. 
Due to the vital role of carbon-containing compounds the cell will not be able to grow or divide without it, 
especially not if carbon doubles as the energy source as well. Carbon enters the central carbon metabolism, 
as shown in Figure 14. The process will be exemplified for glucose, which is a commonly used fermentation 
substrate. Glucose enters the glycolysis, where it is converted into pyruvate, anaerobically. Pyruvate can be 
consumed in two ways at the end of glycolysis: under anaerobic conditions it is fermented into ethanol or 
lactic acid. With oxygen present pyruvic acid can enter the TCA cycle. Of the two pathways, the route 
through the TCA cycle generates most energy, in the form of ATP. The secretion of carbon from the cell is 
very dependent on the pathway it has been involved in. Typical products include: ethanol, organic acids, sec-
ondary metabolites and carbon dioxide (Kampen 2014). 
 
The compounds used as carbon sources varies greatly with application. Simple sugars, like glucose, enter the 
metabolism directly whereas polysaccharides, like starch, needs initial degradation before they can enter the 
central carbon metabolism. Sugar alcohols and lipids can serve as carbon sources for some organism. The 
cell has enzymatic systems which control the utilization of carbon sources. Catabolite repression is the 
mechanism, where the cell uses the most easily utilizable carbon sources first, by up and downregulating the 
expression of enzyme-encoding genes. Catabolite inhibition is a mode of control where the presence of glu-
cose influences the expression level of enzymes in the pathway. Catabolite repression can be said to be anal-
ogous to feedback inhibition in other biosynthetic pathway systems (Kampen 2014). 
 
==PAGE:31==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 31 of 154 
 
Nitrogen   
Nitrogen, in its reduced form, is incorporated in the organic parts of the cell, more specifically; amino acids, 
proteins, nucleic acids and co-enzymes. After carbon, nitrogen is usually the component that is added in the 
second largest quantity to a fermentation medium. The forms of nitrogen, which can be used as nitrogen-
sources varies greatly among organisms; some can utilize inorganic sources, some nitrates and some amino 
acids and protein hydrolysates, like peptone. Ammonium sulphate, peptone and yeast extract are common 
sources of nitrogen (Kampen 2014).  
 
Micro- and trace nutrients  
Other elements are also involved in microbial growth; they are stated, along with their main function(s), in 
Table 5. The minerals and metals are micro- and trace-elements which means that they are only needed in 
small or very small amount in the cell.

1.2.4.2. Fermentation kinetics and models 
Fermentation processes are powerful tools that can broaden the understanding of an organism, a pathway, 
a product or a combination – as described above. To gain that understanding a model is needed. The model 
is the combination of two components: a kinetic model of the biological system and a model for the fermen-
tation vessel.  
 
1.2.4.2.1. A kinetic model of the biological system 
A kinetic model of the production organism should cover the cellular metabolism. Cellular metabolism is 
very complex, but the kinetic model should be as simple as possible without losing vital information. The 
Black Box model (Figure 15) is one such model, it combines all the biochemical reactions, within the cell, into 
one overall reaction; the total conversion of substrate into biomass and products.  
 
 
Figure 15: Visualisation of the Black Box model; substrate enters the cells and products exists it. The model does not take intracellular 
reactions into account. Adapted from Bioreaction Engineering Principles 2nd ed. (Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 2011).  
The only components considered in the Black Box model are:  
Substrate: all components present in the medium as well as supplied gas 
Metabolic products: all secreted products; extracellular macromolecules (enzymes), metabolites and CO2  
Biomass: the pool of macromolecules that the cell is composed of. Biomass is also a product but in the Black 
Box model is it distinguished from the other products 
 
The conversion of substrate into biomass and products occurs at a certain rate; r, unit [h-1]. In Figure 15 the 
relevant rates are shown in grey. rs is the consumption rate of substrate; it describes how fast the substrate 
is taken up by the cell. rp and rCO2 are both formation rates; they describe the formation of products. µ is also 
called the specific growth rate, it describes the rate at which biomass is formed. When the organism grows 
at its highest possible rate, the specific growth rate is denoted µmax. 
 
The Black Box model can also be presented mathematically, where the sum of biomass and products with 
the substrate subtracted should be zero.

N is the number of substrates, M is the number of metabolic products, x is biomass, S is substrates, P is met-
abolic products, Yxp is the yield and stochiometric coefficient of the product per biomass. Unit [gP/gX] or 
[cmoleP/cmoleX], Yxs is the yield stochiometric coefficient of substrate per biomass. Unit [gS/gX] or 
[cmoleS/cmoleX] 
 
The assumptions are, that only one substrate is limiting, the system is at steady state which means that all 
rates and yields are constant. The Black Box model is a powerful tool for the balancing of extracellular com-
ponents (Stephanopoulos, Aristidou, and Nielsen 1998; Nielsen 2004; Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 2011).  
 
1.2.4.2.2.  A model for the fermentor 
 
The model for the fermentor is a set of dynamic mathematical equations for substrate, metabolic products 
(one per product) and biomass. These equations are called mass balances, their purpose is to describe the 
change in concentration of substrate, biomass and metabolic products, over time. The dynamic mass bal-
ances are derived from the mass balance equation:  
 
𝐴𝑐𝑐𝑢𝑚𝑢𝑙𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑑= 𝑁𝑒𝑡 𝑓𝑜𝑟𝑚𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑒+ 𝐼𝑛−𝑂𝑢𝑡 
Where:  
Accumulation: is the rate of change in the compound it describes, Net formation rate: the term is negative 
for the substrate and positive for metabolic products, In: describes the flow of that compound into the fer-
mentor, Out: describes the flow if that compound out of the fermentor 
 
The dynamic mass balances are formulated as differential equations:  
 
𝑆𝑢𝑏𝑠𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑒:   𝑑𝑐.,)
𝑑𝑡= −𝑟.,) ∗𝑥+ 𝐷(𝑐.,)

Where:  
rs,I: Substrate uptake rate, x: Biomass concentration in the fermenter, D: Dilution rate , cfs,I: Concentration of 
substrate in the feed, cs,I: The substrate concentration in the fermenter, rs,I: Product formation rate , Cfp,I: 
Concentration of the product in the feed, csp,I: The concentration of the product in the fermenter, µ: specific 
growth rate 
 
The dynamic mass balances change according to the mode of cultivation as some of the terms can be set 
equal to zero  (Stephanopoulos, Aristidou, and Nielsen 1998; Nielsen 2004; Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 
2011).

Modes of cultivation  
There are three main modes of cultivation for fermentation processes, these are batch, fed-batch and the 
continuous culture (chemostat). Figure 16 illustrates the set-ups.    
 
 
Figure 16: Visualisations of batch, fed-batch and chemostat fermentors.  
Inspired by Bioreaction Engineering Principles 2nd ed. (Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 2011) and Mhairi Workman (Former Associate 
Professor at DTU Bioengineering) 
Batch cultivation 
The batch cultivation is the simplest mode, there is no in- or outflow, the volume remains constant over 
time. The mass balances for batch cultivations are very simple, as the “In” and “Out” terms are eliminated. In 
the dynamic mass balances the dilution rate is zero, and therefore also the term following it.  
 
𝑆𝑢𝑏𝑠𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑒:   𝑑𝑐.,)
𝑑𝑡= −𝑟.,) ∗𝑥 
 
𝑃𝑟𝑜𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡:   
𝑑𝑐&,)
𝑑𝑡= 𝑟&,) ∗𝑥 
 
𝐵𝑖𝑜𝑚𝑎𝑠𝑠:   𝑑𝑐%
𝑑𝑡= µ ∗𝑥 
 
The specific growth rate will always assume its maximum value (µmax) at the beginning of the cultivation, be-
cause nutrients are widely available and waste products have not built up (Stephanopoulos, Aristidou, and 
Nielsen 1998; Nielsen 2004; Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 2011).  
 
Fed-batch cultivation  
Concentrated feed medium is added to the fed-batch fermentor while no broth is removed; the volume in-
creases over time. The feeding allows for a more controlled environment; a constant substrate concentra-
tion can be kept, dilution can eliminate inhibiting compounds and high titers can be achieved. The dynamic 
mass balances for fed-batch cultivations are identical to the general ones. As the volume and the feeding 
rate increases, the terms in the equations need to be dynamic (Stephanopoulos, Aristidou, and Nielsen 1998; 
Nielsen 2004; Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 2011).  
 
==PAGE:35==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 35 of 154 
 
Continuous culture/chemostat 
A chemostat has a feed inlet and a waste outlet, both operated at the same rate; as pictured in Figure 16. 
The chemostat will therefore have a constant volume. A chemostat is limited, partly by only having one limit-
ing substrate and partly by the dilution rate, which the specific growth rate will be equal to. A chemostat cul-
ture can achieve steady-state where all fluxes within the cell will be constant, the concentrations of sub-
strate, biomass and products will be the same in the fermentor as in the outflow.  
 
𝑆𝑢𝑏𝑠𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑒:   0 = −𝑟.,) ∗𝑥+ 𝐷(𝑐.,)
M −𝑐.,)) 
 
𝑃𝑟𝑜𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡:   0 = 𝑟&,) ∗𝑥+ 𝐷(𝑐&,)
M −𝑐&,)) 
 
𝐵𝑖𝑜𝑚𝑎𝑠𝑠:   0 = (µ −𝐷) ∗𝑥 
 
The result is no change over time in substrate, products and biomass; the mass balances can therefore be 
set equal to zero (Stephanopoulos, Aristidou, and Nielsen 1998; Nielsen 2004; Villadsen, Nielsen, and Lidén 
2011).   
 
1.2.4.2.3. The BioLector® I Micro-fermentation system 
 
The BioLector® I system is a high throughput micro-fermentation system from the company m2p labs (Figure 
17). It can run 48 parallel batch fermentations in a microtiter plate with 2.4 mL wells, it monitors biomass 
concentration (unit: Light Scattering Units (LSU)), pH, dissolved oxygen and fluorescence with intervals of 
less than 20 minutes. The system can adjust and maintain the temperature in the chamber, ensure mixing 
and agitation by shaking the plate, minimise evaporation due to sophisticated humidity control. (“M2p-Labs 
| Home” 2018; m2p labs 2015).

can be seen. Biomass and florescence is measured through the clear bottom of the plate by a light source (m2p labs 2015).  
The BioLector® I system has numerous applications; in synthetic biology it allows fast, easy and accurate 
comparison and characterisation of the constructed organism. For cloning experiments the BioLector® deliv-
ers a powerful high-throughput screening method as it can measure both biomass and florescence. The high 
frequency of measurements makes the BioLector® a valuable tool in media optimisation experiments, where 
high-throughput is also key (“M2p-Labs | Home” 2018; m2p labs 2015).

An advantage that makes the BioLector® I extremely powerful is that the results are scalable to lab-, pilot- 
and production-scale fermenters. The microtiter plates for the BioLector® system has a very unique design: 
see Figure 17, which allows a broad range of defined oxygen transfer rates (OTRs). Furthermore, some of the 
plates have optodes that allow for pH and dissolved oxygen measurements, these are also shown in the fig-
ure (“M2p-Labs | Home” 2018; m2p labs 2015).  
 
1.2.5. Optimisation strategies for media optimisation  
 
Optimisation strategies are very different based on their application. Some methods are associated to con-
struction of a heterologous cell factory, others are molecular biology tools used to enhance the production 
of a compound in a native producer. What these have in common is that they alter the genome of the organ-
ism by genetic engineering. (Davy, Kildegaard, and Andersen 2017). 
A whole other category of optimisation techniques, and the focus of this section, revolves around media op-
timisation. Here, no alteration is done to the cell, but intracellular fluxes are redirected by adjusting the 
availability and type of nutrients and by changing process parameters (Singh et al. 2017).  
 
1.2.5.1 Medium and process condition optimisation  
Medium optimisation is a critical step in the establishment of a new production process. The main motiva-
tion for successful media optimisation is to reduce the cost of a given process to gain revenue. Decreasing 
production cost is done by optimising titer and substrate utilisation (yields) as much as possible. For bulk 
chemicals or low-value-high-volume products it is even more important to maximise the production and 
lower cost, as the profit window is smaller. One of the challenges is strain-to-strain variability when it comes 
to specific nutrient requirements. The type of product and genetic alterations (if any) do also alter the re-
quirement of the organism. Therefore no media composition suits all organisms even though they are re-
lated. Media optimisation is therefore needed in almost all cases (Singh et al. 2017).  
 
1.2.5.2 Optimisation strategies   
 
Classical methods  
The most common classical method is the “One-Factor-at-a-time” (OFAT) method, which was used almost 
exclusively until the 1970ties. In OFAT one variable is chosen and varied at a time, while the other variables 
are held constant. The chosen media component or process parameter is tested over a range of values. 
When the optimum is established, the concentration or parameter is held constant, in the next round of ex-
periments another component or parameter should be chosen as the variable. The procedure is repeated 
with all contributing factors. The main advantage of OFAT is its simplicity in all aspects. The experiments are 
easy to set up, as only one parameter is changed for every run. No statistical knowledge or specialised com-
puter programmes are needed to analyse the output data. All results can be presented as simple graphs. 
OFAT is still the superior method when the organism, and its metabolism, have not yet been characterised.  
The main disadvantage is that a large number of experiments are needed in order to get satisfactory data on 
the optimum. In some cases the global optimum is not even discovered. The large number of experiments 
also induces a high cost in labour, materials and time. Few variables have to be chosen, as it is not possible 
to screen them all. If two of the variables or media components have an interaction it will not be shown in a 
OFAT analysis (Singh et al. 2017; Wahid and Nadir 2013). 
==PAGE:37==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 37 of 154 
 
Statistical methods 
Design of Experiments (DOE) is a term for a large group of statistical frameworks. The general idea is that a 
series of experiments is planned out in a way so that a large amount of information can be retrieved from 
few experiments. Using a reduced number of experiments, is possible as several components or variables 
are varied at the same time and compared. Factorial design is often applied; the variables are set at one of 
two settings; “high” or “low”. The main advantage of DOE is that it reduces the number of experiments 
needed to determine the optima. DOE can detect interaction between factors. One disadvantage is that a 
knowledge on statistics and statistical tools is needed to analyse the more complex data generated. The 
main disadvantage is that the biological system should be characterised beforehand. To set the levels: “high” 
and “low” it is required to know what these are for the system. Another significant disadvantage is the sensi-
tivity towards the dynamics present in biological systems in terms of substrate consumption, production of 
metabolites and pH alterations; DOE requires all non-variable factors to be constant (Singh et al. 2017; 
Wahid and Nadir 2013; Mandenius and Brundin 2008).  
 
1.2.5.3.Parameters for optimisation  
 
Carbon to Nitrogen ratio (C/N ratio) 
The ratio between these two critical media components has an effect on the synthesis of certain compounds 
within the organism. In their study from 2015, Gropoşila-Constantinescu et al. describe C/N ratio as the most 
important factor for lipid accumulation in Y. lipolytica . They found that lipid accumulation is facilitated when 
nitrogen is being depleted in the medium after 24-48 hours of cultivation. Depletion of other media compo-
nents can also trigger lipid accumulation. After nitrogen has been depleted cell proliferation will stop, as crit-
ical proteins can no longer be synthesized. The remaining carbon in the medium will still be taken up by the 
cell and converted directly into lipids, which are then accumulated in the LBs. To reach the maximum lipid 
accumulation, the C/N ratio should be high; around 20 and up. A suitable set-up to accumulate as much lipid 
ad possible is a chemostat where the organism is cultivated at one fourth of its maximum growth rate and 
where the nitrogen concentration is very close to zero. Once the organism is under these conditions it will 
convert the carbon source into lipids. If the cells become carbon limited, at any point, they will start to use 
the storage lipids to drive their metabolism (Gropoşila-Constantinescu et al. 2015). A drawback is that nitro-
gen limited conditions will promote citrate excretion as well as lipid production (Kavšcek et al. 2015).  
 
Carbon source  
The carbon source itself can also alter the lipid metabolism in Y. lipolytica. In the study “Production of Micro-
bial Lipids by Yarrowia lipolytica” from 2015 Gropoşila-Constantinescu et al. investigate the influence of five 
different carbon sources on biomass and lipid production. The carbon sources were: maltose, fructose, su-
crose, lactose and glucose. The results are summarized in Figure 18. The figure shows that glucose is the best 
carbon source based on both biomass and lipid production, followed by sucrose. The other carbon sources 
perform very similarly (Gropoşila-Constantinescu et al. 2015).  
 
==PAGE:38==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 38 of 154 
 
 
Figure 18: The performance for five different carbon sources on biomass production and lipid production. The conditions for the culti-
vations were: 30° C, 30 g/L carbon source, pH 6 and 220 rpm. All values are in [g/L]. Yellow represents biomass and light blue repre-
sents lipid production. Figure taken directly from (Gropoşila-Constantinescu et al. 2015). Original legend from article “Figure 1. Effect 
of carbon sources on biomass and lipid production”.  
Another study “Optimization of lipid production with a genome-scale model of Yarrowia lipolytica ” by Ka-
všček et al. compared the lipid production and accumulation with glucose and glycerol as sole carbon 
source. When glycerol was the sole carbon source 71.8 mg/gDW of lipids was produced, compared to 75.8 
mg/gDW for glucose (Kavšcek et al. 2015). Also the concentration of the carbon source has an impact on 
both biomass and lipid formation. If the initial carbon concentration is too high it will inhibit the biomass 
production. On the other hand, a low concentration will have a negative effect on the volumetric lipid pro-
duction (Mathiazhakan Kuttiraja et al. 2016).  
 
pH value 
Yarrowia has a high adaptive capacity and can therefore survive under alkaline or acidic conditions. The cells 
do, however, experience alkaline stress that lead to an increased level of mitochondrial ROS and thereby in-
creased activity of the enzyme superoxide dismutase that eliminates ROS in the cell. Fluctuations in pH in 
both batch and chemostat cultures does not affect the viability of Y. lipolytica as it has a high tolerance to-
wards pH stress (Timoumi et al. 2017). Lipid production is favoured in the high end of acidic range of the pH 
scale. Several studies have showed that pH 5-6.5 is optimal. It was also proven that the best lipid production 
was seen when the pH was kept constant using pH control in the vessel. At lower pH values the concentra-
tion of lipids dropped by over 40%. The pH range between 5.5 and 6.5 is also the optimum for citric acid pro-
duction. Around pH 7 and up will induce a filamentous morphology in Y. lipolytica (M. Kuttiraja, Dhouha, and 
Tyagi 2017; S. Papanikolaou et al. 2002).  
 
Oleic acid addition 
Oleic acid (C18H34O2) is a monounsaturated omega-9-fatty acid (Figure 19).   
 
Figure 19: The structural formula of Oleic acid (C18H34O2).  
Created in ChemDraw version 16.0.1.4

OH
==PAGE:39==

Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 39 of 154 
 
 
Oleic acid is the most abundant fatty acid in nature, where it is the major component in plant oils; especially 
in olives and almonds, where it makes up about 70-80% of the fat content. The oil is colourless to slightly 
yellow and odourless.  The density is 0.89 and it is insoluble in water (PubChem 2018). An increase in lipid 
accumulation can be seen in Y. lipolytica when oleic acid is used as a co-substrate at 3% (v/v). The accumula-
tion occurs simultaneously with the other primary growth activities (Beopoulos et al. 2008).   
 
Dissolved oxygen availability and limitation 
Yarrowia has a respiratory metabolism and is Crab-tree negative, unlike S. cerevisiae that has an overflow 
metabolism, which renders it capable of producing ethanol in the presence of oxygen. These factors lead to 
higher biomass yields in Yarrowia when oxygen is in excess. It has been confirmed that oxygen limitation will 
lead to induced lipid synthesis in Y. lipolytica. In a study from 2015, Kavšcek et al. found that the oxygen 
demand decreases when the lipid synthesis was initiated. Upon this discovery they proved that lowering the 
oxygen avaliability could induce lipid producion in a batch fermentation. Once a certain biomass 
concentration had been reached, they reduced the areation from 1 vvm to 0.4 vvm yielding a oxygen 
saturation the in medium of 1%. The result was an increase of lipids in the biomass by 80% and the yield of 
lipids was improved more than four-fold compared to a standard experiment (Kavšcek et al. 2015).

2. Experimental strategy  
 
The aim of the experiments in study thesis is to optimise the production of beta-carotene in the heterolo-
gous strain, Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, both in terms of titer, yield and production cost. The overall strategy 
is divided into four phases, each with a set of experiments and an aim. The strategy is outlined in Figure 20 
below.  
The majority of the optimisation will be based on minimal medium as it facilitates the possibility to do more 
sophisticated calculations. The reminder will be based on complex media or complex media components.  
 
 
 
Figure 20: Overview of the experimental strategy of this thesis. The strategy is divided into four phases. Each phase contains experi-
ments and has an aim; the experiments are stated on top and the aim at the bottom. 
2.1. Phase 1 
In this phase, initial preparation will be done. Cryo stocks will be prepared, to ensure that the same mono-
culture of cells is used through all experiments. As Larroude et al. did not include characterization in their 
study “A synthetic biology approach to transform Yarrowia lipolytica into a competitive biotechnological pro-
ducer of β-carotene” (Larroude et al. 2017) this thesis sets out to uncover the main morphological traits of Y. 
lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF.  
 
2.2. Phase 2 
The aim of Phase 2 is to determine the base-line of growth and beta-carotene production of Y. lipolytica ob-
CHCTEFCTEF, thereby creating a basis for comparison and evaluation of the optimisations that will be carried 
out later in the strategy. The experiment will be a simple batch experiment using the minimal medium 
Delft/CBS. The experiment has two functions; the first is to deliver data for comparison throughout the the-
sis work, the other is to add to the sparse knowledge on the characteristics of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF in 
terms of specific growth rate, carbon consumption, yields, beta-carotene concentration and by-product for-
mation. A comparison to the strain Y. lipolytica Y3820 will be carried out. Y. lipolytica Y3820 was constructed 
by Dr. RodrIgo Ledesma Amaro (Imperial College London). The comparison will be based on biomass deter-
mination, HPLC measurements and simple carbon balancing.  The values that will be measured and evalu-
ated upon will be pH, dissolved oxygen, biomass concentration (by dry weight and optical density), beta-car-
otene concentration, glucose concentration and by-product concentration in both the gas and liquid phase 
in the fermentor.   
 
2.3. Phase 3 
This phase contains initial screening of strategies, which could optimise the production of beta-carotene. To 
ensure a high throughput, micro-fermentations will be carried out in microtiter plates (MTPs). The m2p-labs 
==PAGE:41==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 41 of 154 
 
BioLector® I will be the workhorse in this phase, as it can run 48 scalable fermentations in parallel. The val-
ues that will be measured and evaluated upon in this phase are: biomass (LSU), biomass concentration [g/L] 
and beta-carotene concentration [g/L].For the optimisation a OFAT approach will be used, as it is the most 
suitable for a unknown biological system. The parameters that will be tested for optimisation are based on 
those described in the section “1.2.5.3. Parameters for optimisation”. In summary the strategies are: carbon 
to nitrogen ratio, carbon source, pH value and oleic acid addition. Each of these parameters have been 
tested experientially in other studies and have proven to improve lipid synthesis or lipid accumulation in Yar-
rowia. As thoroughly described, the biosynthetic pathway in Y. lipolytica  ob-CHCTEFCTEF is linked to the lipo-
genesis as the pathways share a common pool of acetyl-CoA. The parameters were chosen as it was hypoth-
esised that they will direct the flux of carbon in the central carbon metabolism to favour the lipogenesis and 
thereby increasing the availability of acetyl-CoA, which could ultimately be used for the synthesis of beta-
carotene. A range of values for each strategy, will be tested. Furthermore, a complex medium will be tested. 
As described higher specific growth rates and biomass concentrations are reported for complex media. The 
complex medium will give an indication of the plausibility of an industrial production. On the contrary the 
complex medium cannot be used to determine the effect of C/N ratio, as its components are not clearly de-
fined. Each strategy will be evaluated based on six parameters; specific growth rate, final biomass concentra-
tion, the yield of biomass on substrate (Ysx), final beta-carotene concentration, the yield of beta-carotene on 
substrate (Ysp) and the yield of beta-carotene on biomass (Yxp).  
 
2.4. Phase 4 
The fourth and final phase sets out to find the optimal set of parameters that yields most product, either di-
rectly based on amount or relative to cost. After the initial screenings in Phase 3, the first step of Phase 4 will 
be to evaluate all of the results and determine the optima for each strategy. From this knowledge experi-
ments, with various combinations of optima, will be designed. The first round of cultivations will be carried 
out as micro-fermentations like in Phase 3.  
The best (one or several) strategy will be tested in 1L bioreactors. The single best strategy will be applied to 
a 10L bioreactor to test industrial robustness and scalability. The values measured and evaluated upon are 
the same as in Phase 2.
