3.4. Cryostocks of Y. lipolytica  strains 
An 80% glycerol solution was prepared from ≥99% glycerol for molecular biology (Sigma Aldrich, Germany) 
and ddH2O. The solution was autoclaved at 121 °C for 20 minutes. From stock-plates the Y. lipolytica ob-
CHCTEFCTEF or Y3820 cells were streaked onto fresh YPD plates (Table 8) and incubated lid down for 72 hours 
at 30 °C.  
To 500 mL shake flasks, 100 mL of liquid YPD was added (Table 9). 100 µL of sterile vitamin solution (Table 
12) and 100 µL of sterile antifoam (204, Sigma Aldrich, Germany) were added before inoculation with single 
colonies from YPD plates. The flasks were incubated at 28 °C and 150 rpm. After 23 hours of incubation 200 
µL of culture was mixed with 800 µL 80% glycerol solution in cryo tubes. The tubes were stored at -80 °C.  
 
3.5. Pictures of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF 
 
3.5.1. Microscope pictures 
Pictures were taken of 24 h liquid YPD culture (Table 9); undiluted and 2x diluted with ddH20. Pictures were 
also taken of colonies from a YPD plate suspended in ddH20.  Pictures were taken with a Nikon eclipse E1000 
microscope and Image-Pro Plus (v. 5.2.1.59) with the following settings; for 40x magnification the exposure: 
90 ms and for 100x magnification the expose was 600 ms.   
 
3.5.2. Macromorphology pictures 
Pictures were taken of YPD plates inoculated from cryostocks, that had been incubated at 28 °c for 48 hours 
and 72 hours. The pictures were taken with the 12 megapixel dual-pixel digital camera of Samsung Galaxy s7 
set to standard settings.

3.7. Fermentor experiments – Batch – CBS medium 
Batch fermentations were carried out with standard CBS medium for base-line determination, characterisa-
tion of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, and comparison to Y. lipolytica Y3820. Optimised CBS-medium was applied 
for evaluation of the performance of the two optimised media compositions for Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. 
The experiments were conducted as triplicates. The fermentors (Sartorius Stedim Biostat Q Plus 
(Sartotius Stedim, Germany)) were fitted with a baffle cage. pH and dissolved oxygen probes were fitted and 
two-point calibrated according to the manufacturers description. CBS media (standard and optimised) were 
prepared from stocks (Table 10, Table 11) according to Table 13 and Table 14. Two optimised media were 
prepared; one containing 1% oleic acid and one with 0% oleic acid. For experiments containing oleic acid, 
organic olive oil was used. The olive oil had a content of 72% pure oleic acid. Concentrations were based on 
the amount of pure oleic acid. The fermentors were autoclaved at 121 °C for 20 minutes with the salt-based 
medium and the carbon source held separately. Vitamin solution (Table 12) and olive oil was added after au-
toclaving.

After autoclaving the process parameters were adjusted to the following; aeration: 0.75 vvm, stirring: 800

2013).  
 
3.7.1. Sampling procedure  
For dry weight determination: samples were filtered through membrane filters (pore size: 0.45 μm, Monta 
Mil® PES, Frisenette, Denmark) using a vacuum pump. The filters were washed twice with ddH2O. The filters 
were dried for 20 minutes in a microwave (180 watt) and placed in a desiccator. For HPLC; samples were fil-
tered (pore size: 0.45 μm, Q-Max®, Frisenette, Denmark) into HPLC vials and stored at -20°C. For chemical 
analysis; approximately 1 mL of crude broth was poured into Eppendorf tubes and stored at -20°C. For OD600 
measurement; the absorbance of crude fermentation was measured at 600 nm in a spectrophotometer. Off-
==PAGE:46==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 46 of 154 
 
gas was continuously analysed by mass spectrometry (MS) (Thermo Scientific, model: Prima Pro, Cheshire 
United Kingdom). 
 
3.7.2. Sugar analysis - High Performance Liquid Chromatography  
Samples were prepared for HPLC analysis as described in “3.7.1. Sampling Procedure” above. During the 
analysis the samples were kept at 4 °C. The injection volume was 20 µL, the eluent was 5 mM H2SO4 and the 
flow rate was 0.6 mL/h. A Bio-Rad Aminex HPX-87H column (CA, USA) was used and kept at 60 °C.  
The standard for the characterisation experiments contained the following; glucose (20 g/L), glycerol (2 g/L), 
acetate (2 g/L), ethanol (20 g/L) succinate (0.5 g/L) and pyruvate (0.5 g/L). The standard for experiments with 
optimised medium contained: glycerol (20 g/L), citric acid (1 g/L) and acidic acid (1 g/L). Appendix 1A shows 
chromatograms of both standards. 
The Refractive Index Detector and DAD of the Hewlett Packard 1100/Agilent 1200 HPLC system were applied 
for the quantification of the metabolites.  Data analysis was carried out in Agilent Chemstation.  
 
3.8.BioLector® I micro-fermentations  
 
3.8.1. Media for BioLector® I experiments 
Appendix 2 shows a table with all micro-fermentations carried out in this thesis. For C/N ratio, oleic acid ad-
dition and YPD medium experiments the amounts of carbon source and nitrogen sources are listed. Where 
nothing else is mentioned the standard amounts (Table 13) were used. For micro-fermentation experiments 
with CBS medium the stock solutions described in Table 10, Table 11 and Table 12 were used. For experi-
ments with standard concentrations of the media components, and varying pH, a standard CBS medium was 
prepared (Table 13 with no antifoam) and pH was then adjusted with 2M H2SO4 and 2M KOH to yield each 
pH value. For experiments with varying C/N ratio and type of carbon source, stock solutions  (Table 10, Table 
11, Table 12) were used. Each stock solution was adjusted to pH 5 and held separately. The concentration 
and thereby amount of carbon and nitrogen sources were calculated based on the number of moles of pure 
carbon and pure nitrogen. An example of a calculation can be seen in Appendix 3. The concentration of the 
other media components were held constant in all experiments.  For experiments with YPD medium stocks 
were prepared according to Table 15. For experiments containing oleic acid, organic olive oil was used. The 
olive oil had a content of 72% pure oleic acid. Concentrations were based on the amount of pure oleic acid.  
Potassium hydrogen phthalate was used as pH buffer in all experiments.

3.8.2. Loading the plate 
The BioLector FlowerPlate® was sterilised using 70% ethanol. The total volume of each well was 1500 µL; 
1450 µL medium and 50 µL inoculum. The prepared media were added to the wells; media for experiments 
with varying pH had been pre-mixed, for media with varying C/N ratio and oleic acid addition each media 
component was added individuallyInoculum was prepared in baffled shake flasks, using CBS medium with 
the same carbon source as in the plate. The shake flaks were inoculated with a single colony of Y. lipolytica 
ob-CHCTEFCTEF and incubated at 28° C and 150 rpm for 24h. The cells were washed twice with ddH20. Be-
tween each wash step the cells were centrifuged at 3000 rpm for 15 minutes. After the second wash step 
the cells were re-suspended in ddH2O. The plate was inoculated to yield a initial OD600 of 0.01.   
 
3.8.3. Process conditions and equipment 
All process conditions and equipment used can be seen in Table 16. At least 90 minutes before the experi-
ment was started the BioLector® I was set to warm up and calibrate. The warm up programme was identical 
to the conditions stated in Table 16; cycle time was set to 30 minutes. Initial screening experiments were 
terminated after 72h and optimised medium experiments after 50h.

3.9. Extraction and analysis of beta-carotene content  
Beta-carotene was retrieved from the cells by chemical extraction and quantified by HPLC.  
 
3.9.1. Solvents  
Extraction solvent: 50:50 v/v Heptane (Sigma Aldrich, Germany) and Ethyl Acetate (Sigma Aldrich, Germany) 
with 0.01% Butylated Hydroxytoluene (Sigma Aldrich, Germany) was prepared. HPLC solvent: 99.9% ethanol 
(Kemetyl, Køge, Denmark) was mixed with 0.01% Butylated Hydroxytoluene (Sigma Aldrich, Germany). Both 
solvents were stored in the dark. 
 
3.9.2. Extraction of beta-carotene from liquid samples 
Micro-centrifuge tubes with 200 µL of crude liquid sample were placed in liquid nitrogen for 5-10 seconds 
before being placed in a freeze-dryer (Heto DRYWINNER, retailer: Holm&Halby, Brøndbyvester, Denmark) 
for 2 hours.  Approximately 500 µL of glass beads (710-1180 microns, Sigma St. Louis, USA) and 500 µL of ex-
traction solvent were added to the tubes. Tubes were vortexted for approximately 30 seconds, before 1 mL 
of additional extraction solvent was added. Tubes were placed in a sonicator (Branson 2510 Ultrasonic 
==PAGE:48==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 48 of 154 
 
Cleaner, Branson, CT, USA) for 20 minutes. Tubes were then centrifuged at maximum speed for 5 minutes. 1 
mL of the extract was transferred to new tubes. The extraction solvent was evaporated under nitrogen in the 
dark. The extract was re-suspended in HPLC solvent and transferred to HPLC vials for light sensitive products.  
 
3.9.3. High Performance Liquid Chromatography method 
3.9.3.1. Standard curve construction 
A ³97% pure Beta-carotene standard (Sigma Aldrich, Germany) was dissolved in HPLC solvent at a concen-
tration of 29.6 mg/L. In the HPLC (Agilent 1100 series) a standard curve was constructed, see Figure 21. 
Chromatograms of the standard represented in Appendix 4A.  
 
 
Figure 21: Standard curve for Beta-carotene measured at 450 nm.  
The curve was constructed by Laboratory technician Andreas H. R. Heidemann (DTU Bioengineering) 
For the analysis a Kinetex® C8 LC column with 2.6 µm silica particles (Phenomenex, CA, USA) was used. The 
system was run in gradient mode at a flow rate of 0.3 mL/min; solvent A was methanol/milli-Q water with 50 
mM ammonium acetate (95%), solvent B was methanol (5%). The pressure was set to minimum 60 bar. The 
gradient was set to 8 minutes and equilibration for 4 minutes. The column oven was kept at 60° C. The diode 
array detector was set to detect beta-carotene at 450 nm3.

3.10. Data analysis  
All specific growth rates and yields were calculated in Microsoft Excel.  
 
3.10.1. Determination of specific growth rate using dry weight measurements (batch) 
For calculation of the specific growth rate data points from the exponential growth phase were used. Bio-
mass concentration [g/L] was log-transformed and plotted against cultivation time. A linear trend-line was 
fitted to the graph. The slope is the specific growth rate.  
 
3.10.2. Determination of specific growth rate using OD600 and LSU measurements  
OD600/LSU data points from the exponential growth phase were plotted against cultivation time. An expo-
nential trend-line was fitted to the data and the exponent is the specific growth rate.   
 
3.10.3. Determination of specific growth rate using CO2 from off-gas4  
Data from the process MS (Thermo Scientific, model: Prima Pro, Cheshire United Kingdom) was logged as 
percentages of all gasses and volatile compounds in the off-gas with corresponding time points. 
The percentages were transformed into rates and accumulated CO2 in g/L; the calculations are shown in Ap-
pendix 5.  
For determination of the specific growth rate, accumulated CO2 [g/L] (exponential growth phase) was log-
transformed and plotted against cultivation time.  A linear trend line was fitted; the slope is the specific 
growth rate.  
 
Examples of growth rate determination from experimental data is presented in Appendix 6A. In tables all 
growth rates will be displayed with standard deviations. In plots showing cultivation courses the error bars 
represent the standard deviation. In plots for comparison the error bars signify the upper and lower 95% 
confidence intervals. The underlying statistical calculations along with examples are presented In Appendix 
7.  
 
3.10.3. Yields and carbon balancing  
Yields were calculated for both BioLector® and fermentor experiments, using different methods. Data for 
yields were retrieved from dry weight determination (biomass concentration), off-gas MS (accumulated CO2) 
and HPLC (substrate and products).  
 
In fermentor experiments all yields were determined on substrate-basis from production and consumption 
rates, as the following apply:  
𝑌)Q =
𝑟Q [𝑔𝑗∗𝐿V- ∗ℎV-]
𝑟) [𝑔𝑖∗𝐿V- ∗ℎV-] 
 
When multiple data points are known the yield can be determined from a plot. In Microsoft Excel product 
concentration (biomass, CO2, beta-carotene) was plotted against substrate concentration for data in the ex-
ponential phase. Each curve was equipped with a linear trend-line, the slope of which is the yield.

3.11. Treatment of data from the BioLector® I 
To transform the LSU data from the BioLector to a format of data that could be analysed as described under 
“Determination of specific growth rate using OD600 and Light Scattering Units measurements” it was run 
through a R script. The script was coded and modified by Associate Professor Christopher Workman (DTU 
Bioengineering) to fit the specific data in this thesis. The code can be found in Appendix 10 were the function 
of each line has been explained.  
The general purpose of the script was to remove outliers and remove background noise from the instru-
ment.

4. Results 
 
4.1. Characterisation of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF 
 
4.1.1. Morphology  
From cryostocks Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF was streaked unto YPD plates and incubated at 28 °C. Pictures 
were taken of the plates after 48 hours and 72 hours, see Figure 22 below.  
 
Colonies on YPD – 48 hours 
Colonies on YPD – 72 hours 
 
 
Close-up of colonies on YPD – 72 hours 
 
Figure 22: Colonies of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF on solid YPD medium. Pictures were taken after 48 hours (top left panel) and 72 
hours (top right and bottom panel). 
On YPD the colonies appear heavily convoluted, mat and vax-like. The colour is orange, the intensity appears 
higher after 72 hours than after 48 hours. As seen in the close-up in Figure 22 the colonies grow on the sur-
face of the medium.  
 
Black and white microscope pictures were taken of cells from a YPD plate suspended in ddH20 and of a liquid 
24h YPD culture. The pictures were taken with 40x and 100x magnification. For the liquid YPD medium pic-
tures were taken of both 2x diluted broth and undiluted broth. The pictures are shown in Figure 23. The cells 
from the plate, display filamentous growth; only few of them are unicellular. The exponentially growing cells 
from the liquid YPD medium are all single cells; many of which are budding. The LBs can be identified as 
small white circles in the cells. The colour of the LBs could not be determined from the pictures as they are 
black and white, they did however, appear orange in the ocular.

4.1.3. Physiological characterisation in batch fermentations with CBS medium 
 
 
Triplicates with two strains of Y. lipolytica were run using a standard CBS-glucose medium and standard con-
ditions for Yarrowia in 1L fermenters. The aim was to thoroughly characterise the new strain Y. lipolytica ob-
CHCTEFCTEF (beta-carotene producer) and subsequently compare its performance to that of its parental strain 
Y. lipolytica  Y3820 (lipid overproducer).Samples for dry weight, OD600 and HPLC were taken approximately 
every 1.5 hour in the exponential phase, starting when CO2 levels reached 0.1%. For Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEF-
CTEF samples for OD600 and HPLC were taken with larger intervals in the subsequent growth phases. Off-gas 
was continuously analysed by MS and process parameters logged in MFCS/win. Metabolite concentrations 
were measured by HPLC-analysis against a standard. Beta-carotene concentration was determined by HPLC 
analysis using a standard reference material, selected chromatograms from the analysis are presented in Ap-
pendix 4B. Prior to analysis the beta-carotene was chemically extracted from the cells.  
 
All data for DW, OD600, glucose concentration, background corrected CO2 (multiplied by a factor 20) and 
beta-carotene concentration (multiplied by a factor 20) can be seen in Figure 24. Each line represents a 
mean of the triplicates (except for beta-carotene data were one replicate was discarded) with error bars rep-
resenting the standard deviation to each side. Appendix 11 presents plots with the underlying data for the 
triplicates. All raw data can be found by following the link shown in Appendix 22.  
 
 
Figure 24: Combined fermentation data for triplicate batch cultivations with Yarrowia lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF in standard CBS me-
dium. For each curve the mean was found for the triplicates (duplicate for beta-carotene concentration)) and error bars based on the 
standard deviations  were fitted. NOTE that background corrected CO2 has been multiplied by a factor 20 to show the resemblance to 
DW and OD600. Beta-carotene concentration has also been multiplied by a factor 20.

From Figure 24 it can be noted that lag phase lasted from inoculation to 13.40 hours of cultivation. It can 
also be observed that growth curves for biomass concentration, OD600 and CO2 followed each other very 
closely. After the lag phase the cells grew exponentially until around 37 hours where all the glucose was con-
sumed as seen in the yellow graph, which shows the glucose consumption. As soon as the glucose has run 
out, CO2 production dropped by 80% and a short four-hour plateau (from t=37-41 hours) in cell growth was 
seen. The short plateau contains the exit from the exponential phase along with the stationary phase. The 
stationary phase was very short, as it can be noted that OD600 and CO2 already start to decrease at 41 hours.  
After the stationary phase the cells entered the death phase where CO2 and OD600 declined continuously. 
From 60 hours of cultivation until the end, the CO2 curve stagnated a little over the background CO2 percent-
age. Only one exponential growth phase was seen. From the standard deviations it can be noted that the 
triplicates differ, especially for beta-carotene concentration.  
 
The increase in beta-carotene concentration follows the increase in cell growth. In the stationary and death 
phases the concentration of beta-carotene decreased at the same rate as OD600. To visually investigate the 
evolution of the colour and thereby beta-carotene production over time, pictures were taken and the colour 
was described. The results can be seen in Figure 25.  
 
Cultivation time [hours]

Orange-ish yellow

Figure 25: Pictures and descriptions of colour evolution in CBS cultivations with Y. lipolytica. The pictures shown are representative to 
the triplicates.  
CBS medium is colourless and therefore the colour at the start of the fermentation was transparent with a 
hint of yellow, due to the inoculum. Figure 25 shows that the colour of the bioreactor changed from a pale 
yellow at 21 hours colour to a dark, bright orange at 61 hours. Little change was seen between 36 h and 61 
h. The change in colour is in accordance with the beta-carotene production and increase in biomass concen-
tration over time that was seen in Figure 24.

In regards to the percentage of CO2, Figure 26 shows the same trends as Figure 24 as they originate from the 
same data. CO2 increased exponentially for 37 hours before it reached its maximum value of approximately 
1.7%. As the percentage of CO2 increased the dissolved oxygen concentration decreased from 98% to 55%. 
When the percentage of CO2 dropped vertically from its maximum to 0.3% the dissolved oxygen concentra-
tion increased rapidly.

4.1.3.2. Yields and carbon balancing  
In the HPLC analysis of the samples, the most predominant species was glucose. None of other known extra-
cellular metabolites were seen in detectable amounts. At the time of the analysis it was noted that no uni-
dentified peaks were seen, but it was not possible to further investigate the chromatograms, as they were 
lost as the result of a virus attack on the computer for HPLC analysis. 
 
Yields on gram-basis were calculated directly based on data from dry weight determination, accumulated 
CO2 and HPLC analysis. Data from DW, CO2 and beta-carotene was plotted against substrate concentration in 
the exponential phase, the yield was the slope of the linear trend line. The plots are presented in Appendix 
8B. Yields on cmole basis were found by multiplying by the molecular weight of the respective compounds. 
Underlying calculations are shown in Appendix 9. Table 19 presents yields for each replicate on gram and 
cmole basis. In the bottom row the yields are converted into a carbon balance, the numbers given are the 
deviation from zero in percent.  For triplicate 1, data on beta-carotene concentration was discarded as it was 
inconsistent.  
For the numbers on gram-basis the R2 values from the plots are included.

8B and 9.

ing the link shown in Appendix 22.  
 
 
 
Figure 27: Combined fermentation data for duplicate batch cultivations with Yarrowia lipolytica Y3820 in standard CBS medium. For 
each curve the mean was found for the duplicates and error bars were fitted. NOTE that background corrected CO2 has been multi-
plied by a factor 20 to show the resemblance to DW and OD600. 
From Figure 27 it can be noted that the graphs for cell growth (DW, OD600 and background corrected CO2) 
follow the same trend very closely. The lag phase last for approximately 7.5 h (data not shown). The cells 
grow exponentially for approximately 18 hours before entering the stationary phase and death phase. The 
entry into the stationary phase can be seen as the CO2 percentage drops as soon as the glucose is depleted 
from the medium. As for Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF the stationary phase only serves as a few hours of transi-
tion into the death phase.  
 
Figure 28 below show the comparison of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF (red line) and Y3820 (black line) in regard 
to OD600, biomass concentration, accumulated CO2, glucose concentration and background corrected CO2.

The differences in growth phases can be noted from OD600 (top left), glucose concentration (middle right) 
and background corrected CO2 (bottom left); the lag-phase of strain Y3820 is 7.5 hours compared to 13.40 
hours for strain ob-CHCTEFCTEF. The exponential growth phase of Y3820 is also shorter; 18 hours compared to 
23 hours. This is visualised especially on the glucose consumption, which is faster and has a higher slope for 
Y3820. Entry into stationary and death phase is very similar for both strains; the stationary phase is very 
short and followed by death phase rather than a second exponential growth phase. The slopes of all graphs 
are steeper for strain Y3820 than for strain ob-CHCTEFCTEF, the error bars to not overlap.  The biomass con-
centration reaches the same approximate level at the end of the fermentation, OD600 is slightly higher for Y. 
lipolytica  ob-CHCTEFCTEF. Carbon dioxide production is higher in Y. lipolutica Y3820 than in Y. lipolytica ob-
CHCTEFCTEF.

duplicate, the underlying plots are shown in Appendix 6B. For Y. lipolytica  ob-CHCTEFCTEF the mean specific 
growth rates are shown, originating from Table 18.

fermentation. Representative chromatograms are shown in Appendix 1B.

4.2. BioLector micro-fermentations with Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF 
 
In the BioLector® I, micro-fermentations were performed to screen media compositions and pH values for 
optimisation of beta-carotene production, specific growth rate (µ), biomass concentration, the yields of bio-
mass and beta-carotene on substrate and the yield of beta-carotene on substrate. Biomass concentration 
was based on dry weight measurements and beta-carotene concentration was based on chemical extraction 
and HPLC analysis. Selected chromatograms from the analysis are presented in Appendix 4B.  
The BioLector® I can run 48 micro-fermentations in parallel. The FlowerPlate system ensures aeration and 
agitation comparable to that in lab-scale bioreactors. In this this study all experiments were carried out as 
triplicates with a fixed temperature of 28 °C, humidity control enabled and shaking at 1000 rpm.  
The data was treated in R (version 3.4.4) and Microsoft Excel as described in “Treatment of data from the 
BioLector”.  
 
In  Figure 29 below an example of a 48 well BioLector™ FlowerPlate™ can be seen. In the plate Y. lipolytica 
ob-CHCTEFCTEF was cultivated under the conditions stated in the table in the lower panel.  
 
 
 
 
 Figure 29: An example of a BioLector 48 well flower plate with Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF (upper panel) under the conditions stated in 
the table in the lower panel. Each cell in the table refer to a well in the plate. All experiments were run I triplicates, starting from left 
to right. Well F6-H6 are reference wells used for the comparison of different plates.

4.2.1. Micro-fermentations with CBS-glucose medium 
 
4.2.1.1.  Comparing pH values (CBS-glucose) 
pH values between 3 and 10 were screened to find the optimum based on the parameters described. 
Growth profiles can be seen in Figure 30 below. Each line represents a triplicate. Data from 0-18 hours is not 
shown.  
 
 
Figure 30: Light scattering data from micro fermentation with varying pH values. pH values ranged between 3 and 10. The light scat-
tering units measured by the BioLector are  plotted against cultivation time for all values. Each line represent a triplicate – each plot is 
shown in Appendix 13.  
From Figure 30 it can be observed that no growth was detected at pH 3 or pH 10. For pH 4 slow growth was 
seen and a lag phase of over 48h. At pH 5 (standard pH for Yarrowia cultivations) a higher degree of growth 
could be observed, than for the more acidic pH values. At pH 5.5 the growth curve followed that of pH 5 in 
the beginning, hereafter the slope becomes steeper. The slope of the graph for pH 6 was steeper than pH 5 
and pH5.5. Growth at pH 7 and 8 seemed almost identical; they had the same period of lag phase and the 
same slope in the exponential phase. At pH 8 the exponential growth phase was a little longer than at pH 7. 
Linear growth and a smaller slope was observed at pH 9.  
 
The specific growth rate was determined from LSU data in the same manner as from OD600, an example can 
be seen in Appendix 6A, the remaining plots are presented in Appendix 13. The presented growth rates are 
the mean of triplicates with the associated 95% upper and lower confidence interval. For each fermentation 
the specific growth rate was determined along with the final biomass concentration, using the filtration 
method, and the concentration of beta-carotene. Biomass concentration and beta-carotene concentration 
were determined from one representative replicate, due to low capacity for analysis. From the measured 
data, yields for biomass on substrate (Ysx), beta-carotene on substrate (Ysp) and beta-carotene on biomass 
(Yxp) were calculated. For yield calculation it was assumed that all carbon-source was depleted from the me-
dium. See Figure 31 for summary of the optimisation parameters.

Figure 31: Summary of micro-fermentations with CBS-glucose medium with varying pH values. Data in table form is presented in Ap-
pendix 13.  Dark blue bars represent the specific growth rate [h-1]. Purple full line is the biomass concentration [g/L]. Orange full line is 
beta-carotene concentration [g/L]. Yellow dashed line is the yield of beta-carotene on substrate [gBetaCarotene/gGlucose]. Light blue 
dashed line is the yield of beta-carotene on biomass [gBetaCarotene/gDW]. Green dashed line is the yield of biomass on substrate 
[gDW/gGlc]. Red error bars represent the 95% confidence interval for the specific growth rate.  
From the presented data it can be noted the optimum for the specific growth rate, biomass concentration 
and Ysx was pH 8 (0.191±0.91 h-1, 6.367 g/L, 0.318 gDW/gGlc), followed by pH 7 (0.189±0.005 h-1, 6.126 g/L, 
0.306 gDW/gGlc). The confidence intervals for the specific growth rates overlap. pH 6 presented the third 
highest values for growth rate, biomass concentration and Ysx (0.168 h-1 , 5.349 g/L, 0.268 gDW/gGlc). From 
the highest values around pH 6-8, the specific growth rate, biomass concentration and Ysx decreased to-
wards both the more acidic and more alkaline pH values. The highest values for beta-carotene concentration 
(0.410 g/L), Ysp (0.021 gBC/gGlc) and Yxp (0.147 gBC/gDW) were observed for pH 5.5 followed by pH 6 and pH 
7. The six parameters for optimisation was divided into two distinct groups: biomass-related parameters had 
the highest values in the acidic-to-neutral pH values whereas beta-carotene concentration related parame-
ters have the highest in the neutral-to-alkaline part of the pH scale. The lowest values for all tested parame-
ters were observed at pH 3 and pH 10.  
 
The final biomass concentration for pH 5.5 appears low (2.8 g/L) compared to the adjacent values, therefore 
the yields that include biomass will be high. The value might be an outlier.

4.2.1.2.Comparing Carbon/Nitrogen ratios (CBS-glucose) 
C/N ratios between 0 and 17.75 were screened to find the optimal ratio. The calculations for determination 
of the ratios were based on moles of pure carbon and pure nitrogen, rather than the amount of glucose-
monohydrate and ammonium sulphate. The C/N ratio in standard CBS-glucose medium is 1.78; 20 g/L glu-
cose and 5 g/L ammonium sulphate. The lowest C/N ratio (0) represents 0 g/L of glucose and the standard 5 
g/L of ammonium sulphate. The highest C/N ratio (17.75) represents 200 g/L, which is ten times the stand-
ard amount, ammonium sulphate concentration was kept at 5 g/L. An example of a calculation is shown in 
Appendix 3. Growth profiles can be seen in Figure 32 below. Each line represents a triplicate.  
 
 
Figure 32: Light scattering data from micro-fermentations with varying C/N ratios, using glucose as the sole carbon-source. The C/N 
ratio ranged between 0 and 17.75. The light scattering units measured by the BioLector are plotted against cultivation time for all 
ratios. Each line represent a triplicate- each plot is shown in Appendix 14. 
From Figure 32 it can be observed that very little or no growth was seen at C/N 0 and C/N 0.0071 (no carbon, 
no nitrogen respectively). A linear growth profile with a small slope was seen for C/N 17.75. The graphs for 
the remaining experiments look similar; they had between 30 and 35 hours of lag-phase and similar slopes. 
C/N 1.78 differed slightly, as it had a shorter lag-phase. 
 
Specific growth rate, final biomass concentration and beta-carotene concentration were determined and the 
yields were calculated, the values can be seen in Figure 33.

Figure 33: Summary of micro-fermentations with CBS-glucose medium with varying C/N ratios. Data in table form is presented in Ap-
pendix 14 . Dark blue bars represent the specific growth rate [h-1]. Purple full line is the biomass concentration [g/L]. Orange full line is 
beta-carotene concentration [g/L]. Yellow dashed line is the yield of beta-carotene on substrate [gBetaCarotene/gGlucose]. Light blue 
dashed line is the yield of beta-carotene on biomass [gBetaCarotene/gDW]. Green dashed line is the yield of biomass on substrate 
[gDW/gGlc]. Red error bars represents the 95% confidence interval of the specific growth rates.  
From the data presented it can be noted that the lowest values for all six optimization parameters were ob-
served at C/N 0 (no carbon) and C/N 0.0071 (no nitrogen). Only minor variation was seen in the specific 
growth rates for C/N ratios ranging from 0.18 to 8.88, the error bars overlap for all of these specific growth 
rates. The specific growth rate decreased to 0.092±0.002 at the highest C/N ratio of 17.75 (200 g/L glucose).  
For the biomass concentration another trend was observed; for C/N 0.18-0.89 the concentration was stable 
around 5 g/L. At C/N 1.78 (standard C/N ratio) the biomass concentration was 3.85 g/L and thereby one 
gram lower than at the lower and higher C/N ratios. For the higher C/N ratios, C/N 3.55-17.75, the biomass 
concentrations ranged between 7.285 g/L (C/N 7.1) and 9.323 g/L (C/N 8.88).  
The highest yield of biomass per substrate was seen for C/N 0.36 (0.293 gDW/gGlc) followed by C/N 0.89 
(0.267 gDW/gGlc) and C/N 0.18 (0.253 gDW/gGlc). The relationship between the amount of glucose and the 
relative production of biomass was not linear. As well as for the yield of biomass on substrate, the beta-caro-
tene concentration and the yields concerning it, had their optima in the lower C/N ratios. The highest beta-
carotene concentration was 0.295 g/L (C/N 0.18) followed by 0.160 g/L (C/N 1.78) and 0.140 g/L (C/N 0.36). 
The exact same order of the three highest scoring C/N ratios was seen for Ysp and Yxp.  The C/N ratios from 
C/N 3.55 and above performed poorly compared to the lower C/N ratios in terms of beta-carotene concen-
tration, Ysp and Yxp.

4.2.2. Micro-fermentations with CBS-glycerol medium 
 
4.2.2.1. Comparing pH values (CBS-glycerol) 
The experiments were carried out just as described above for experiments with glucose. pH values ranged 
between 3 and 10. The visual appearance of the culture broth can be seen in  Figure 29, which is placed right 
under the headline “4.2. BioLector micro-fermentations”. The average growth profiles, from triplicates, are 
units plotted against cultivation time in Figure 34. Data before t=15 hours is not shown.  
 
 
Figure 34: Light scattering data from microfermentations performed in CBS-glycerol medium with varying pH values. pH values 
ranged between 3 and 10. The light scattering units measured by the BioLector are  plotted against cultivation time for all values. 
Each line represent the mean of a triplicate – each plot is shown in Appendix 15. 
The following observations can be made from Figure 34; no growth was observed at pH 3 or at pH 10. The 
graphs for pH 6, pH 7 and pH 8 wre similar; they had the highest slopes of the experiments and also the 
shortest lag-phases; of the three pH 7 had the highest slope. pH 9 had a constant slope and a longer lag-
phase.  
 
All data for specific growth rate, final biomass concentration, beta-carotene concentration and the yield of 
biomass on substrate (YSX) are summarised in Figure 35. The specific growth rate was determined from tripli-
cates, biomass and beta-carotene concentrations were determined based on representative replicates. For 
yield calculation it was assumed that all carbon-source was depleted from the medium

Figure 35: Summary of micro-fermentations with CBS-glycerol medium with varying pH values. Data in table form is presented in Ap-

From the presented data it can be noted that the specific growth rate was highest at pH 7 (0.262±0.019 h-1), 
pH 6 (0.25±0.003 h-1), pH 5.5 (0.246±0.003 h-1) and pH 8 (0. 0.225±0.016 h-1) where the confidence intervals

more acidic or alkaline environments. The highest beta-carotene concentration of 0.594 g/L was observed 
for pH 7 and was followed by pH 8 (0.531 g/L) and pH 5.5 (0.498 g/L). The highest Ysp was reported at pH 7 
(0.030 gBetaCarotene/gGlycerol). The second highest (0.027 gBetaCarotene/gGlycerol) and third highest

was observed; the highest value (0.089 gBetaCarotene/DW) was observed for pH 7, followed by pH 5.5 
(0.080 gBetaCarotene/DW) and pH 8 (0.068 gBetaCarotene/DW). At pH 3 and pH 10 no biomass was de-
tected either when looking at specific growth rate or biomass concentration. These observations are in ac-
cordance with the visual appearance of the broth that can be seen in  Figure 29. Wells A1-C1 and A4-C4 rep-
resent pH 3 and pH 10 respectively; the medium is clear indicating no growth. No visual difference in colour 
intensity can be seen between wells G1 to E3 that represent the range of pH values between 5 and 8 (both 
included).

4.2.2.2. Comparing Carbon/Nitrogen ratios (CBS-glycerol) 
The calculations for determination of the ratios were based on moles of pure carbon and pure nitrogen, ra-
ther than the amount of >99% glycerol and ammonium sulphate. The C/N ratio in standard CBS-glycerol me-
dium is 7 (20 g/L glycerol and 5 g/L ammonium sulphate). The higher standard C/N ratio in glycerol-based 
medium than in glucose based medium is due to lower molecular weight of glycerol and thereby more car-
bon per gram-basis. The lowest C/N ratio (0) represents 0 g/L of glycerol and the standard 5 g/L of ammo-
nium sulphate, the experiment was identical to that carried out with glucose as the sole carbon-source. The 
highest C/N ratio (7) was the standard medium composition.  
 
Growth profiles for the cultivations can be seen in Figure 36 below. Each line represent a triplicate. The part 
of the lag-phase before 20 hours of cultivation is not shown.  
 
 
Figure 36: Light scattering data from microfermentations with varying C/N ratios, using glycerol as the sole carbon-source. The C/N 
ratio ranged between 0 and 7. The light scattering units measured by the BioLector are plotted against cultivation time for all ratios. 
Each line represent a triplicate – each plot is shown in Appendix 16. 
From Figure 36 it can be observed that no growth could be detected at C/N ratios of 0 and 0.028. For the re-
maining experiments the lag phase was approximately 25 h. Of the remaining experiments C/N 0.875 had 
the lowest slope, C/N 7 had the highest. The ratios C/N 5.25 and C/N 7 had identical growth profiles from 0h 
to 35 h where they branch out.  
 
All data for specific growth rate, final biomass concentration, beta-carotene concentration and the yield YSX 
are summarised in Figure 37. Specific growth rate was determined from triplicates, biomass and beta-caro-
tene concentrations were determined based on representative replicates. For yield calculations it was as-
sumed that all carbon-source was depleted from the medium

From the data presented it can be noted that the highest specific growth rate (0.229±0.003 h-1) was ob-
served for the C/N 7. The second (0.219±0.002 h-1) and third (0.193±0.002 h-1) highest specific growth rate 
were observed for C/N 5.25 and C/N 3.5, respectively. Very low growth rates were seen when no nitrogen or 
no carbon was added to the medium. The highest final biomass concentration was 6.789 g/L (C/N 7), the 
second highest (6.131 g/L) was seen for C/N 0.875. When accounting for the concentration of glucose and 
looking the at yield the highest value of 0.343 gDW/gGlycerol was reported for C/N 5.25, followed by C/N 7 
(0.340 gDW/gGlycerol) and C/N 3.5 (0.335 gDW/gGlycerol). The highest beta-carotene concentration ob-
served was 0.385 g/L (C/N 0.875) followed by 0.357 g/L (C/N 7) and 0.209 g/L (C/N 3.5). The Yxp values follow 
this pattern exactly with and optimum at C/N 0.875 of 0.063 gBetaCarotene/DW. Ysp had its optimal value of 
0.021 gBetaCarotene/gGlycerol at C/N 3.5 followed by 0.019 gBetaCarotene/gGlycerol (C/N 0.0875) and 
0.018 gBetaCarotene/gGlycerol (C/N 7).  
 
4.2.2.3. Examining the effect of oleic acid (CBS-glycerol) 
Oleic acid was added as olive oil, which contains approximately 72% oleic acid on weight basis (DTU Food 
2018). Olive oil was added in amounts that yielded 1% (10 g/L), 2% (20 g/L), 3% (30 g/L) and 4% (40 g/L) pure 
oleic acid. pH was adjusted to 5. The visual appearance of the culture broth can be seen in Figure 29 from 
well D4 to G5 (both included). The colour of the broth in the four experiments appear similar.   
 
The average growth profiles from triplicates, for the four oleic acid concentrations, are plotted as light scat-
tering units plotted against cultivation time is shown in Figure 38. The first 15 hours of lag phase are not

acid added compared to that with 1% and 2%. Moreover the yields for the 2% oleic acid medium, 
were twice as high as those for 3% and 4% addition.

4.2.3. Micro-fermentations with YPD-glucose/glycerol medium

Vitamins and trace metals were added in some cultivations. To determine whether a single media compo-
nent was limiting, experiments were carried out with double the amounts of carbon source in one and dou-
ble the amount of the other media components in another. pH was kept constant at 5.5. In Figure 29 the cul-
tivations with double the amount of glycerol and yeast extract/peptone respectively, can be seen in wells 
H5-E6.

each plot is shown in Appendix 18. . 
From Figure 40 it can be noted that all the graphs follow the same trend; a lag-phase of approximately 20 
hours and a steep slope.

on the specific growth rate. The specific growth rates range between 0.211±0.013 h-1 and 0.232±0.014h-1,

with supplements)

4.3.1. Micro-fermentations 
The BioLector system was set to the same physical parameters for the optimised media as for the screening: 
28°c, 1000 rpm and humidity control enabled. The four media compositions all had glycerol as carbon source 
in a C/N ratio of 0.875. pH was either 7 or 8. Oleic acid, as olive oil, was added to half of the media in a con-
centration corresponding to 1% pure oleic acid.  
The growth profiles of the experiments can be seen in Figure 42. Each line represents the mean of triplicates.  
 
 
 
Figure 42: Light scattering data from micro-fermentations with optimised medium. Common to all cultivations: the carbon source was 
glycerol and the nitrogen source was ammonium sulphate in a C/N ratio of 0.875. pH was adjusted to either 7 or 8. Oleic acid was 
added to half of the cultivations in a concentration of 1%. The light scattering units measured by the BioLector® I  are plotted against 
cultivation time for all ratios. Each line represent a triplicate – each plot is shown in Appendix 19. 
 
The beforementioned interference in the signal, for oleic-acid containing medium, can also be observed for 
the optimised medium. It can also be noted that those experiments have a higher off-set. The medium at pH 
7 with no oleic acid presents the shortest lag-phase and steepest slope of the four. pH 8 with no oleic acid 
had the longest lag-phase and also the least steep slope. The last two media composition both contained 
oleic acid, one at pH 7 and the other at pH 8. These two follow each other relatively closely in terms of both 
lag-phase and steepness of the slope. The growth parameters are summarised in Figure 43.

4.3.2. Lab-scale fermenters 
In 1L lab scale fermentors the two highest performin media compositions were compared; glycerol as carbon 
source in a C/N ratio of 0.875 at pH 7. One medium contained olive oil corresponding to 1% pure oleic acid, 
the other contained 0% oleic acid. The media are denoted CBS-opt-OA (without oleic acid) and CBS-opt+OA 
(with oleic acid). The process conditions were; 28°C, 800 rpm and pH control using 2M H2SO4 and 2M KOH.  
A comparison the two optimised media compositions in terms of growth characteristics is presented in Fig-
ure 44. The data presented for the optimised medium without oleic acid is based on triplicates. To the 
graphs error bars are fitted, they describe the 95% confidence interval of the specific growth rates.. The un-
derlying data can be found in Appendix 20. The optimised medium with oleic acid is only based on a single 
replicate, as the two others in the triplicate-series failed due to severe foaming.

From the plots presenting OD600 vs. cultivation time and background corrected CO2 vs. cultivation time it can 
be deduced that the lag-phase in both media was approximately 10 hours, followed by 15 hours of exponen-

the stationary phase was initiated, rapidly followed by the death phase for CBS-opt-OA, based on CO2 and 
dry weight. For CBS-opt+OA the CO2 percentage decreased slower and OD600 increased at a lower rate after 
initiation of the stationary phase. DW and OD600 values were higher for CBS-opt-OA than for CBS-opt+OA.

5. Discussion 
 
5.1 Studies on beta-carotene production by Yarrowia lipolytica   
Studies from literature, besides the work of Larroude et al., describe heterologous beta-carotene production 
in Y. lipolytica. In 2017 Gao et al. constructed a strain that produced 4 g/L of beta-carotene in a rich YPD me-
dium (Gao et al. 2017).   A research team from the Novo Nordisk Center for Biosustainability at the Technical 
University of Denmark has engineered Y. lipolytica to produce beta-carotene (titer: 797.1 ± 57.2 mg/L) and 
the high value carotenoid; astaxanthin (titer: 10.4 ± 0.5 mg/L) (Kildegaard et al. 2017). In 2015 the compa-
nies DuPont and Microbia had already chosen Y. lipolytica as their production platform for the synthesis of 
carotenoids. Microbia has also engineered Y. lipolytica to produce beta-carotene on an industrial scale. 
Safety studies, from Microbia, showed that the produced beta-carotene is safe for human consumption (Zhu 
and Jackson 2015). The titers for the industrial producers are kept enclosed by the companies.  
 
5.2. Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF – Physiological characterisation  
  
5.2.1. Determining the localisation of beta-carotene  
The absorbance at 600 nm and 450 nm was measured for a 24h CBS-glycerol shake flask culture before and 
after filtration for cell removal. From Table 17 it can be noted that there is a difference between the unfil-
tered and filtered broth; the unfiltered both absorbs 700 times more than the filtered. The orange colour, 
and thereby the beta-carotene, must therefore be present only in the biomass. This can be further sup-
ported by the fact that filtered broth appears completely clear or identical to medium whereas the filter ap-
pears orange. Furthermore, the hydrophobic LBs within the cells, did also appear orange in the microscope, 
from the high concentration of beta-carotene. These results support the findings by Larroude et al. 
(Larroude et al. 2017).  
 
5.2.2. Batch cultivations – Standard CBS-glycerol medium 
Batch fermentations in CBS medium with glucose as carbon source were carried out for strains Y. lipolytica 
ob-CHCTEFCTEF and Y. lipolytica Y3820 in 1L fermentors. Partly to do a characterisation of strain Y. lipolytica 
ob-CHCTEFCTEF and partly to compare it to its parental strain. The characterisation will be discussed first, fol-
lowed by the comparison.  
 
The strain Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF did not display a diauxic growth pattern when glucose was used as sole 
carbon source. Figure 24 shows a dramatic decrease in CO2 production as soon as glucose had been de-
pleted. The stationary phase was very short, only a little CO2 was produced suggesting poor conditions for 
the cells and little ability to utilize products or the energy accumulated in the LBs or as beta-carotene. The 
stationary phase only served as a short, four hour, transition period from the exponential growth phase to 
the death phase.  Throughout the rest of the experiment the level of CO2 was slightly higher than the back-
ground CO2 which suggests that a fraction of the cells sustained their metabolism from the cell debris of 
lysed cells. Investigation of the HPLC chromatograms showed that no detectable amounts of compounds 
were produced and then consumed. These findings suggest that glucose is metabolised to biomass, CO2, li-
pids and beta-carotene. The absence of a stationary phase along with no catabolism could be due to the fact 
that the genes responsible for mobilisation and beta-oxidation of the TAGs in the lipid LBs (TGL4 and POX1-
6) have been deleted in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF.  
==PAGE:79==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 79 of 154 
 
 
In Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF the production of beta-carotene increased with cell growth; beta-carotene must 
therefore be a growth related product. This hypothesis is further supported as the beta-carotene concentra-
tion also decreases with decreasing cell density. Beta-carotene was only quantified in some samples due to 
low capacity for analysis. From the data available it can be observed that the sample with the optimal cell 
density did also present the highest beta-carotene concentration. In Figure 25, the colour of the fermenta-
tion broth was most intense after 60 hours of cultivation, which was in the death phase. Most likely because 
a lot of cells had ruptured and the beta-carotene had leaked into the medium. The beta-carotene would 
then be easier to see, as the line of sight would not be obstructed by the cell membrane. An observations 
that support this statement is that filtered broth appeared yellow/orange in colour in the death phase, 
whereas they were colourless in the exponential growth phase.  
 
Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF does not suffer from oxygen limitation in the described set-up. The data in Figure 
26 shows that the dissolved oxygen concentration in the medium never dropped below 55%. The respiratory 
metabolism of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF is therefore not compromised in a batch set-up with CBS-glucose 
medium.  
 
5.2.3. Comparison of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF and Y. lipolytica Y3820 
The graphs for comparison of the two strains in Figure 28 have been fitted with error bars that signify upper 
and lower 95% confidence intervals. There is a statistically significant difference where the error bars do not 
overlap.  
Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF and Y. lipolytica Y3820 differ on several parameters, the first of which is the spe-
cific growth rate. Y. lipolytica Y3820 is 0.04 h-1 faster than Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. The explanation is most 
likely the genetic engineering which has been performed. The inserted genes for beta-carotene production, 
are not that big in size. They might, however, affect the growth rate, as much carbon is required to synthe-
sise beta-carotene; 40 carbon atoms per molecule. The carbon channelled into the mevalonate pathway and 
deposited as beta-carotene cannot be utilised in the synthesis of new cells. The trade-off is the gain of a cell 
factory, that produces beta-carotene but grows at a lower specific growth rate than its parental strain.   
 
The yield of biomass on substrate (Ysx) is very similar, around 0.4 g/g, for the two strains, when cultivated in 
CBS medium with glucose as the carbon source. As Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF produces beta-carotene the 
composition of its biomass has to differ. It was not possible to quantify the quantity of lipid products, in this 
thesis, but it can be hypothesized that the biomass of Y. lipolytica  Y3820 would contain more lipids than Y. 
lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, that on the contrary would contain beta-carotene, along with its precursors and the 
responsible enzymes. The yield of CO2 on substrate (YsCO2) was the highest for Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, 
most likely because CO2 is split out in the mevalonate pathway or the MEP pathway. The difference in the 
CO2 yield is around 0.1 g/g.A lot of carbon is therefore converted into a by-product with no value by Y. lipo-
lytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. It would be desirable to have a lower yield of CO2 and utilise that carbon or other pur-
poses.  
 
The carbon balances presented in Table 21 also differ among the two strains; common to them is that the 
balancing does not return zero as the result. The deviation from zero suggests that some metabolites were 
present, but not quantified by HPLC. The molecular weight for biomass that was used in the yield calcula-
==PAGE:80==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 80 of 154 
 
tions, is a standard value for yeast. As the strains in this study overproduce lipids and synthesise beta-caro-
tene, the biomass should have a lower molecular weight than the standard value. Through analytical meth-
ods it is possible to determine the exact molecular weight of the yeast. The correct molecular weight of the 
biomass could also aid future calculations.  
 
The confidence intervals in Figure 28 do not overlap. All differences are therefore significant at a 95% confi-
dence level. It can therefore be concluded that Y. lipolytica has a significantly faster growth and substrate 
consumption in CBS-glucose medium.  
 
5.3. Designing the optimal medium based on micro-fermentations 
The results of the micro-fermentations from the BioLector® I will be evaluated, in this section, to determine 
the optimal medium composition for optimization of beta-carotene production in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, 
based on the six optimisation parameters. In the end the optimal medium composition will be proposed, 
based on the discussion.  
 
5.3.1. Evaluating the combined effect of pH value and carbon source  
Two carbon sources, glucose and glycerol, were tested along with nine pH values for each carbon source. 
The results are presented in the heat map in Table 23, which is a combination of Figure 31 and Figure 35. 
The blue colour describes the performance of each experiment in relation to the others, for each parameter; 
the blue colour intensifies with increasing values.

likely be pH 7, because the values obtained for pH 8 for final biomass concentration and Ysx are only margin-
ally higher than those for pH 7. Literature describes that pH 5.5-6.5 promote lipid production and accumula-
tion and therefore it was suspected that the higher flux of carbon into the lipogenesis in a wild type Y. lipo-
lytica, would lead into a higher flux towards beta-carotene in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF which seems to be 
the case. The pH range optimal for lipid production and accumulation is also optimal for the production of 
citric acid. Commonly, yeast cultivations are performed at acidic pH values but as Y. lipolytica is resistant to 
pH stress. It is not therefore not surprising that growth was observed for the pH valuespH 4 to pH 9 (both 
included). Little to no growth was seen at the most acidic (pH 3) and alkaline (pH 10) pH values. That growth 
is seen over a large range of pH values, and optimal around the neutral values. Hyphal formation is induced 
at pH values around 7 which could potentially pose an issue, but further experimentation will be needed in 
order to determine the extent of the problem. To be sure to find the true optimal media composition, both 
pH 7 and pH 8 should be tested.   
 
In terms of carbon source glycerol proved to be superior to glucose in CBS medium, based on specific growth 
rate, final biomass concentration, Ysx, beta-carotene concentration, Ysp and Yxp. In the cultivations with YPD 
medium no difference was seen in specific growth rate, however biomass concentrations and beta-carotene 
concentrations were marginally higher for glycerol cultivations. The theory presented in this thesis describes 
that higher specific growth rates are observed when glycerol is the carbon source. The better growth on 
glycerol is based on a higher number of proteins involved in glycerol transport and a more advantageous en-
try into the metabolism of Y. lipolytica. The results obtained in this study are therefore in accordance with 
the literature. The optimised medium should be based on glycerol rather than glucose.

5.3.2. Evaluating the combined effect of C/N ratio and carbon source 
CBS medium with glucose or glycerol was also tested at different C/N ratios to determine the optimal ratio. 
For glucose the C/N ratios between 0 and 17.75 were tested; for glycerol, between 0 and 7. The heat-map in  
Table 24 is a combination of  Figure 33 and Figure 37.  
 
Table 24: Heat-map for micro-fermentations with varying carbon-nitrogen ratios for the two carbon sources: glucose and glycerol. 
The table is a combination of Figure 33 and Figure 37. The blue colour describes the values for each parameter; dark blue signifies 
high value and white signifies the lowest value. The table is constructed in a Danish version of Microsoft Excel and therefore (,) means 
(.) 
 
 
The blue colour in the heat-map in Table 24 is most intense for the values obtained with glycerol-containing 
medium, this supports the beforementioned statement regarding that glycerol is superior to glucose. The 
specific growth rate increased with increasing C/N ratio for CBS-glycerol. The beta-carotene concentration 
and its related yields had their optimal values at C/N 0.875. The overall optimum value was found to be C/N 
0.875. At this ratio the highest beta-carotene concentration and Yxp were reported. Ysx  and Ysp were lower 
than for some of the other ratios (C/N 7 and C/N 3.5 respectively), but only slightly. C/N 7 could also be 
tested to determine its potential.The medium with C/N 0.875 had the standard amount of glycerol (20 g/L) 
and eight times the standard value of nitrogen (equal to 40 g/L of ammonium sulphate). According to litera-
ture lipid production and accumulation should be facilitated by a high C/N ratio. A nitrogen limitation should 
initiate lipid accumulation in the cells. The most efficient way to achieve optimal accumulation should be in a 
chemostat set-up, which could not be tested in the available BioLector® I system. The findings in this thesis 
suggest that beta-carotene production does not increase when the C/N ratio is high, on the contrary, it de-
creased from 0.297 g/L at the lowest C/N ratio (C/N 0.18) to 0.010 g/L at C/N 17.75, for glucose cultivations.  
 
It could be hypothesized that lipid accumulation is still favoured over the mevalonate pathway in Y. lipolytica 
CHCTEFCTEF as it is based on Y. lipolytica Y3820 which was constructed to produce and accumulate lipids.

Figure 45: Figure 1 from “A synthetic biology approach to transform Yarrowia lipolytica into a competitive biotechnological producer 
of β-carotene” (Larroude et al. 2017). 
In strain Y3820 the gene DGA2 was up-regulated (Figure 45). DGA2 catalyses the fusion of DAG and acyl-CoA 
to form TAG. It is therefore likely in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF that DGA2 has a higher rate of conversion than 
the ones to the mevalonate pathway, the flux of carbon to the lipogenesis will be higher than that to meva-
lonate. It could therefore be hypothesized that acetyl-CoA will be consumed to lipid storage. The only one 
pathway leading from TAG to free fatty acids through the steps of mobilisation and beta-oxidation to Acetyl-
CoA is deleted in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. Once acetyl-coA has been converted into TAG it cannot re-enter 
the free pool. As beforementioned the irreversible step catalysed by HMG-CoA reductase is rate-limiting in 
the synthesis of terpenoids. This exact same reaction is also critical in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, as it is the 
reaction that diverts Acetyl-CoA from the lipogenesis to the mevalonate pathway. A possible solution to this 
will be described in the section “7.3.1. Optimising Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF for improved beta-carotene pro-
duction”. 
   
Nitrogen was added as ammonium sulphate which, besides from nitrogen, also contains other elements like 
hydrogen, oxygen and most importantly sulphur. Sulphur is essential to the cell, in small concentrations, to 
synthesise amino acids that contain sulphur and can create di-sulfide bonds. The effect of the higher 
amounts added in order to reach C/N 0.875 were not examined in this thesis.Despite the uncertainties de-
scribed in this subsection, the C/N ratio in the optimised glycerol-based CBS-medium should be C/N 0.875.  
 
5.3.3. Evaluating the effect of oleic acid addition 
To the standard CBS-glycerol medium, oleic acid was added, as a co-substrate, in increasing concentrations 
from 0-4%, as literature states that adding 3% oleic acid as a co-substrate to a minimal medium will increase 
the lipid accumulation in Y. lipolytica. The heat-map in Table 25 is based on Figure 39.   
 
==PAGE:84==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 84 of 154 
 
Table 25: Heat-map for micro-fermentations with CBS-glycerol medium with varying concentrations (0-4%) of oleic acid. The table is 
based on Figure 39. The blue colour describes the values for each parameter; dark blue signifies high value and white signifies the 
lowest value. The table is constructed in a Danish version of Microsoft Excel and therefore (,) means (.) 
 
 
The medium with 1% of oleic acid performed best in terms of specific growth rate, Ysx and beta-carotene 
concentration. The addition of oleic acid lead to almost three times more biomass than with no addition, 
suggesting a better utilisation of the substrate. When 3% (30 g/L) and 4% (40 g/L) were added the final bio-
mass concentration was above 20 g/L in a medium where 20 g/L of glycerol was added. Oleic acid must 
therefore be recognised as a substrate by Y. lipolytica. As described in the background Yarrowia can utilise 
hydrophobic substrates and the observations are therefore in accordance with literature. More experimen-
tation is needed to determine the relative cost of beta-carotene produced on glycerol and oleic acid. The 
effect, desired effect of oleic acid was not observed in these experiments; the yields of beta-carotene de-
creased rather than increased as expected when oleic acid was added.  It could be hypothesised that, once 
again, the lipogenesis is favoured over the mevalonate pathway, due to the re-arrangement of internal 
fluxes and rate-limiting enzymatic steps. Furthermore, carbon which had been converted to TAGs could not 
be cycled back to acetyl-CoA due to the deletion of the responsible genes.  As oleic acid seems to have a pos-
itive effect on some of the parameters and not on others, the ideal solution would be to create two candi-
dates for the optimal medium design; one containing 1% of oleic acid and one containing no oleic acid. It 
could be advantageous to sonicate the oleic acid to yield a better emulsification effect and thereby an in-
creased availability to the cells.  A comparison in the BioLector® I or lab scale fermenters would then reveal 
the best candidate.

which was also used in the fed-batch fermentation performed by Larroude et al. Glucose and glycerol were 
both tested as carbon source, vitamin and trace minerals were also only added in some experiments to de-
termine their function. In two experiments the amounts of the media components were doubled to investi-
gate the presence of limitations. The heat-map in in Table 26 is based on Figure 41.

5.3.5. The optimised media composition from micro-fermentations 
The optimised medium should be based on CBS medium with glycerol as the carbon source. The pH opti-
mum was determined to be 7, pH 8 will also be tested as it should promising results. The C/N ratio should be 
0.875 as it proved superior based on parameters concerning beta-carotene. To achieve a C/N ratio of 0.875, 
the medium will contain 20 g/L of glycerol and 40 g/L of ammonium sulphate. Two concentrations of oleic 
will need to be tested to evaluate their performance: 0% and 1%.

The four proposed optimised media compositions were tested as micro-fermentations. Figure 43 presents 
the results; the yeast is compromised at pH 8, where less biomass and less beta-carotene are produced. The

CBS-opt-OA overlap with the graphs for CBS-opt+OA for background-corrected CO2, substrate consumption

son rather than a better utilisation of the substrate.  
 
The substrate consumption rate was almost identical for the two media compositions, it can therefore be 
hypothesised that oleic acid (and the other lipids in the olive oil) are used as a co-substrate by Y. lipolytica 
ob-CHCTEFCTEF. As soon as the glycerol had been depleted in the CBS-opt+OA medium the yeast entered the 
stationary phase. There are two possible explanations to this; olive oil cannot be utilised in Y. lipolytica ob-
CHCTEFCTEF without the presence of glycerol or because oleic acid has served as a co-substrate it was either 
depleted before glycerol or at the exact same time. The second explanation is the most likely, as Yarrowia is 
known to thrive in fatty environments as well as be known to metabolise hydrophobic substrates, as de-
scribed in the background.  
 
Citric acid is a small organic acid native to Yarrowia. The optimal pH value for citric acid production is be-
tween 5.5-6.5 according to literature, it could therefore be hypothesised that some citric acid would be pro-
duced by Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF in the optimised medium. High production of citric acid is not desirable as 
is consumes nutrients and energy, which could otherwise have been used for biomass or beta-carotene pro-
duction. Citric acid will also acidify the medium, which firstly require addition of more base, e.g. potassium 
hydroxide, to maintain neural pH, and secondly can stress the cell. The extent of citric acid production in the 
optimised medium was therefore elucidated. Samples from the two optimised media were analysed by HPLC 
against a standard containing 1 g/L of citric acid. No citric acid was detected in the analysis. Inspection of the 
spectra at the RT for citric acid showed no peaks, Appendix 1B shows representative chromatograms and 
Appendix 1A shows the standard. Citric acid is therefore not produced by Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF when 
cultivated in optimised medium.  
 
The olive oil did not seem to have any significant positive effect. All of the results for CBS-opt+OA are based 
on one single replicate. The experiment was conducted as triplicates, but after 14 hours of cultivation the 
medium started to foam excessively, and antifoam was added. After 15 mL of antifoam and three more 
hours two of the replicates had to be terminated after. The foaming was properly caused by an unknown 
product of the degradation of olive oil.  
 
5.4.1. Comparison of optimised medium to Larroude et al. and baseline  
 
In their article “A synthetic biology approach to transform Yarrowia lipolytica into a competitive biotechno-
logical producer of β-carotene” the authors carried out some preliminary experiments with Y. lipolytica ob-
CHCTEFCTEF in order to gain some knowledge on the strain. One of the aims in this thesis work was to compare 
the findings for the optimised medium to the results of Larroude et al. as well as the base-line CBS-glucose

The highest titer of 6.5 g/L of beta-carotene is the one obtained by Larroude et al. in a fed-batch fermenta-
tion using YPD-medium and a highly concentrated glucose solution as feed. The experiment yielded a bio-
mass concentration of 72 g dry weight. The Yxp value of 0.0090 gBC/gDW for that experiment is also the high-
est in the table. A single replicate of CBS-opt-OA presented a Yxp of 0.088 gBC/gDW, which is close. It would 
therefore be tempting to conclude that the fed-batch experiment was superior, however the Ysp is the low-
est. 900 g of glucose was used to produce the 6.5 g of beta-carotene and 72 g of biomass, the substrate utili-
sation was therefore poor.  
 
==PAGE:88==

Page 88 of 154 
 
The Ysx  values of the CBS-opt-OA medium are not higher than the ones obtained in minimal medium by Lar-
roude et al. when taking the standard deviations into account. When examining the heatmaps in Table 23,  
Table 24 and Table 25 it can be noted that higher Ysp values were reported for the individual optimisation 
strategies; 0.030 gBC/gGly was seen for CBS-gly at pH 7, 0.019 gBC/gGly was seen for CBS-gly with C/N 0.875, 
0.025 gBC/gGly was seen for CBS-Gly 0% oleic acid. What these values have in common is that they are all 
higher than the Ysp values described by Larroude et al. for minimal medium. Some different explanations 
could be given, one would be that the yield of beta-carotene is lower for the optimised medium than for the 
other media. Another is that the analysis of beta-carotene was not sufficient. The concentration of beta-car-
otene was obtained in two different ways; Larroude et al. used spectrophotometry and RP-HPLC was used in 
this study.  Comparing two methods of quantification adds a level of uncertainty. The samples in this study 
were analysed in two runs which were one and a half month apart and using two different columns, as the 
column from the first run was not available for the second. Both were Phenomenex C8 columns. For both 
experiments the same standard was used, the chromatograms of the standards are shown in Appendix 4a. 
The area of the standard for the second run is lower than that of the first run, which could suggest that the 
standard had degraded a little over time. Because the solubility of beta-carotene is low these small varia-
tions will give larger variations in the measured concentrations. The samples from the optimised media ap-
peared the most visually pigmented of all the samples. It is not possible to determine which of the explana-
tions that is the most plausible one. The best approach to finding the most plausible explanation would be to 
run all samples at once against a freshly prepared standard.  
 
In terms of specific growth rate, final biomass concentration and Ysx the optimised media, CBS-opt-OA and 
CBS-opt+OA were significantly superior to the base-line experiments along with all the screened media com-
positions, even YPD. The specific growth rate of the base-line was improved two-fold and Ysx improved by 0.2 
cmoleDW/cmoleC-source.  
 
5.5. Beta-carotene extraction and quantification 
Precise beta-carotene quantification was essential in this thesis in order to design the most optimal medium 
for production. Beta-carotene is, however, not an easy compound to extract or quantify, some of the issues 
have already been touched upon in the previous section. Due to its nature as an antioxidant and its role in 
plants, beta-carotene is sensitive to both light and oxygen. When in contact with either of those substances, 
it will start to degrade and the colour will become brown, rather than red. A robust antioxidant is therefore 
crucial in the extraction process to avoid degradation. During the extraction procedure it is nearly impossible 
to extract all of the beta-carotene, as it sticks to the cell debris.  
 
Another difficulty is the extremely non-polar nature of beta-carotene; it has 40 carbon atoms and no ox-
ygens. Even though HPLC is a very precise and reliable method, designing a beta-carotene specific method 
proved difficult. If an RP column is used, beta-carotene would stick to the column because of the strong in-
teractions with the alkenes. In some cases it would not be possible to remove the beta-carotene from the 
column at all. No interaction would occur on a NP column, due to the lack of polar groups in beta-carotene. 
The best solution is therefore a RP column with short-length alkanes and gradient mode for the solvent. An-
other difficulty in beta-carotene quantification, is the solubility in HPLC-appropriate solvents. In the cells 
beta-carotene is captured in lipid bodies, where the solubility is high, but it is not possible to use lipids in 
HPLC. The solubility is the highest in chloroform; a solvent that could not be used in this thesis, due to its 
==PAGE:89==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 89 of 154 
 
toxicity. The solvent of choice was therefore ethanol, in which the solubility is only 30 mg/L. Dilution of the 
samples is necessary but can add uncertainty to the measurements.  
 
The method developed in thesis proved to be reliable, in relative terms, for each run. The constructed stand-
ard curve had a R2 value of 0.9959, which lies within the 99% confidence interval. In samples from the micro-
fermentations, where no cells were detected based on dry weight measurements or the LSU data from the 
BioLector® I, the measured concentration of beta-carotene was also zero The issue arose when comparing 
different runs and columns. The samples were heavily diluted, in the extraction they were diluted 7.5 times 
and only 10µL was injected into the HPLC. A concentration of 30 mg/L and an injection volume of 20 µL of 
the standard created a shoulder separation, see Appendix 4A for reference. When 15µL was injected the 
shoulder separation disappeared. This kind of behaviour in a standard with only one pure compound is a 
classical sign of solubility issues5. Small variations will therefore give larger fluctuations and thereby bigger 
uncertainty. The exact uncertainty is not yet known as the method has just been developed.  
 
5.6. Feasibility of beta-carotene production in Y. lipolytica  
As mentioned in the introduction the demand for beta-carotene is increasing. The current methods might 
not be able to satisfy that demand in a satisfactory and sustainable way. The main industrial producer D. sa-
lina has a productivity of 0.05 g/L per day. The recovery from the algae includes the use of hexane, a halo-
genated hydrocarbon and treatment of the algae with calcium hydroxide under argon (Rüegg 1981). The vast 
majority of the beta-carotene produced today is produced by classical chemical synthesis from chemicals as 
acetone with yields ranging between 60% and 85%. Besides acetone the chemical synthesis, utilises reagents 
like Phosphorus tribromide (PBr3), KOH and Hydrogen bromide (HBr) (Stern 1958). Some of the chemicals 
mentioned are either harsh, troublesome to dispose of or both.  
 
Production of beta-carotene in a recombinant Y. lipolytica does not require chemical catalysts as the chemi-
cal synthesis does. Yeasts are easy to cultivate and grow faster than algae which is advantageous. Y. lipolyt-
ica is fairly easy to engineer, several research teams have engineered Y. lipolytica to produce beta-carotene 
– independently of each other.  As described numerous times Y. lipolytica prefers glycerol as substrate. Glyc-
erol is a by-product of biodiesel production where 10% of the product is crude glycerol which is a waste 
product. The biodiesel industry continues to grow, and therefore the availability of glycerol is high. The glyc-
erol can relatively easily be refined to achieve a high purity. Glycerol is a very cheap substrate, in 2011 one 
ton of crude glycerol cost around 100 American dollars and refined glycerol cost around 750 American dol-
lars per ton (Quispe, Coronado, and Carvalho 2013). Beta-carotene is already produced in Y. lipolytica by the 
company Microbia. For all of the reasons stated above, production of beta-carotene in Y. lipolytica is a feasi-
ble process.  
 
5.6.1. Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF -  the most suitable cell factory for beta-carotene produc-
tion? 
As of 2017 Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF  had the highest titer of beta-carotene ever reported in a recombinant 
organism. The organism is easy to cultivate, robust, well-known and can use cheap substrates. Furthermore, 
only very few by-products are produced by the yeast. The main industrial producer D. salina produces 0.05 
g/L per day of beta-carotene, which is almost one tenth of the amount produced in the base-line experiment 
                                                             
5 Personal communication with Metabolomics Core Manager Christopher Phippen (DTU Metabolomics Core) 
==PAGE:90==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 90 of 154 
 
in this study, when it is assumed that the fermentation takes one day. Relative to that production Y. lipolyt-
ica ob-CHCTEFCTEF has a huge potential already. As described some companies already use Y. lipolytica for 
beta-carotene production, the titer of these productions are not known, and therefore they cannot be used 
as a benchmark here.   
 
Even though the potential of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF is already quite substantial, it can be improved. In 
terms of media and process design a little more research has to be put into finding the optimal medium 
composition and determine the best cultivation set-up. As beta-carotene has proven to be a growth related 
product a fed-batch set-up would be the most suitable for production, as it allows for high titers and high 
cell densities.  Some genetic modifications would also be beneficial in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. These are 
thoroughly described under “7.3.1. Optimising Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF for improved beta-carotene pro-
duction” but briefly, they describe the reintroduction of the genes responsible for mobilisation of TAG and 
the beta-oxidation of FFA in order to largen the size of the free acetyl-CoA pool.   
 
With the improvements suggested here Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF would be the most suitable host for heter-
ologous production of beta-carotene in the biotechnological industry.  
 
5.7. The potential of BioLector® I in media optimisation studies  
The experimental strategy of this study relied heavily on the BioLector® I micro-fermentation system. Origi-
nally media optimisation have been carried out in shake flasks, which is a very labour costly procedure. To 
run the shake flasks, media needs to be prepared in large quantities, sampling has to be performed with ap-
propriate time intervals and a lot of equipment and space is needed. In this study, the equivalent of 150 
batch fermentations (control experiments not included) were carried out over the the course of 3.5 months. 
An experimental set-up like this requires a high-throughput and automated system, the BioLector® I is such a 
system as it can run 48 parallel scalable cultivations. As described by m2p Labs the BioLector® I has been suc-
cessfully used for optimisation studies in scientific articles. The findings in this study support that infor-
mation. The micro-fermentations proved to be scalable to 1L batch reactors; the specific growth rate of Y. 
lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF in CBS-glucose medium at pH 5 was 0.139±0.002 h-1 and 0.157±0.010 h-1 for BioLector 
and 1L fermentors, respectively.

6. Conclusion 
A partial aim was to characterise Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF and subsequently compare it to its parental strain 
Y. lipolytica Y3820. In conclusion: Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF produces beta-carotene, which is stored in lipid 
bodies and not secreted. The products produced by Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF are biomass, CO2 and beta-
carotene. The strain was able to utilise glucose, glycerol and olive oil as substrate.  It could also be concluded 
hat Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF differ significantly from Y. lipolytica  Y3820 
 
The overall aim of the study was to optimise the production of beta-carotene in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF . 
The optimised medium performed significantly better than the base-line, CBS-glucose batch experiment, and 
the minimal media fermentations carried out by Larroude et al. for three of the optimisation parameters; 
specific growth rate, final biomass concentration and Ysx. For the remaining three optimisation parameters, 
beta-carotene concentration, Ysx and Ypx  there was no significant difference between the optimised me-
dium, the baseline experiment and the findings for minimal medium fermentations by Larroude et al. no 
conclusion could be drawn. Further experimentation is needed.  
 
Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF has great potential as an industrial production host for the heterologous produc-
tion of beta-carotene, compared to the current main producer D. salina.  
 
The findings in this study proved that the BioLector® I micro-fermentation is suitable for media optimisation 
studies. Growth rates were scalable to lab-scale fermentors.  
 
A RP-HPLC-based method was developed for the analysis and quantification of beta-carotene.
