7. Future perspectives  
 
9.1. Re-run of HPLC samples for beta-carotene analysis 
As suggested in the discussion, the HPLC samples from this study should all be run at the same time against a 
freshly prepared standard, to finally determine the performance of the optimised media described.  
 
7.2. Optimisation strategies  
Some optimisation strategies were not possible to test in the high-throughput, micro-fermentation scope in 
this study. Those strategies would therefore need to be tested out to determine their effect on the produc-
tion of beta-carotene. 
 
7.2.1.Oxygen limitation 
Literature states that inducing an oxygen limitation on a Y. lipolytica culture will facilitate lipid production 
and accumulation. The higher flux of carbon to the lipogenesis, should increase the pool of acetyl-CoA, 
which could lead to increased beta-carotene production. Results from this study showed that Y. lipolytica 
ob-CHCTEFCTEF did produce beta-carotene without being oxygen limited. If inducing an oxygen limitation 
would increase the beta-carotene production was not accounted for. The experimental set-up would be 
quite simple and include a batch fermentation. Oxygen limitation should then be induced at a time where 
the cell density was relatively high, but the carbon source was not depleted. By comparing beta-carotene 
concentration data to the results from a control experiment, the effect could be determined.  
 
7.2.2. Nitrogen limitation 
Nitrogen limitation in Y. lipolytica  should induce the conversion of the carbon source into storage lipids, ra-
ther than other cellular metabolites . To investigate if the strategy would affect the beta-carotene produc-
tion, a chemostat experiment should be set-up. The particular experiment is described in the “Background” 
section of this thesis. In brief terms, the yeast is cultivated in a chemostat which a dilution rate that corre-
sponds to one fourth of the organism’s specific growth rate. The nitrogen concentration should be close to 
zero, but never be depleted from the medium. Once again the results should be compared to a control ex-
periment.  
 
7.3. Genetic engineering strategies  
 
7.3.1. Optimising Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF for improved beta-carotene production  
At the time of the publication of “A Synthetic Biology Approach to Transform Yarrowia lipolytica into a Com-
petitive Biotechnological Producer of β-Carotene” in 2017 Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF produced the highest 
amount of beta-carotene ever reported. Further genetic engineering on the strain, could most likely opti-
mise the production even more. Figure 46 shows some suggestions to modifications.  
 
==PAGE:93==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 93 of 154 
 
 
Figure 46: Genetic modifications to optimise beta-carotene production in Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTE. Genes which should be upregu-
lated are green, genes which should be down regulated are blue and genes that should be knocked-out are red. DHAP = Dihydroxy-
acetone phosphate, G-3-P: Glyceraldehyd-3-phoaphate, DAG = Diacelglycerol, TAG = Triacylglycerol, FFA = Free fatty acid, HMG-CoA 
= 3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl-co-enzyme A, GUT2 = Glycerol-3-phosphate dehydrogenase 2, GUT1 = Glycerol kinase 1, , DGA2 = Di-
acylglycerolacyl transferase, TGL4 = Triacylglycerol lipase 4, POX1-6: Fatty-acyl coenzyme A oxidase, GGS1 = Geranylgeranyl pyro-
phosphate synthase, CarRP = Pytoene synthease, CarB = Pytoene dehydrogenase. The figure is a combination of Figure 1 and Figure 7.  
Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF was based on the lipid overproducer Y. lipolytica Y3820, which had been con-
structed in another study. To over produce and accumulate lipids, the gene, TGL4, responsible for mobilisa-
tion of TAGs and the genes, POX1-6, responsible for the beta-oxidation of FFA has all been deleted. The 
genes had not been re-introduced in the construction of Y. lipolytica  ob-CHCTEFCTEF. The result is that all the 
carbon which has entered the lipogenesis and been converted into DAG or TAG will become inert to the cell 
as it cannot mobilise and oxidise it back to the precursor acetyl-CoA. The design of Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF 
was based on the high availability of acetyl-CoA, a lot of which might end in lipid bodies as storage lipids, in-
stead of in the mevalonate pathway. The optimisation strategies in this study were also based on increasing 
the flux towards the lipogenesis, as more acetyl-CoA would then be available. As described the flux towards 
the lipogenesis might be higher than that to the mevalonate pathway, as 3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl-CoA 
reductase (HMG1) is rate limiting. Re-introduction and upregulation of TGL4 and POX1-6 is crucial as it al-
lows for recirculation of carbon from the lipid bodies to the pool of acetyl-CoA, which can then be utilised in 
the mevalonate pathway.  
 
To even further increase the metabolic flux towards the mevalonate pathway HMG1 should be upregulated 
as well. The reaction it catalyses is, as mentioned, rate limiting which means that it is likely to have the high-
est metabolic control over the pathway. Furthermore, the reaction is irreversible which means that once the 
carbon has been converted to mevalonate, it cannot be cycled back into the lipogenesis or the central car-
bon metabolism.  
 
The diacylglycerolacyl transferase, DGA2, should be down regulated, in Y. lipolytica  Y3820, it was initially 
upregulated. Its function is to combine acyl-CoA and DAG to yield TAG which is then stored in the lipid bod-
ies. The acyl-CoA which serves as a substrate for DGA2 is derived from acetyl-CoA. The gene should only be 
downregulated and not knocked out as the could be hypothesised that the lipid bodies protect the cell from 
crystallisation of beta-carotene. As described the solubility of beta-carotene in most compounds is poor, 
==PAGE:94==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 94 of 154 
 
however it is more soluble in fat. Not only will the cell be protected from the beta-carotene, the beta-caro-
tene itself will also be protected from light and oxygen as long as it is enclosed in the lipids.  
 
As concluded in this study glycerol is the superior substrate for Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF, compared to glu-
cose. Glycerol enters the glycolysis at glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate (G-3-P), which is serval enzymatic steps 
after glucose. The genes shown in red in Figure 46 are involved in the glycolysis. The function is to divert G-3-
P into DHAP, the step is not essential to the glycolysis, but it adds a futile cycle of carbon, which could other-
wise have been converted directly into acetyl-CoA. The genes responsible should therefore be knocked-out 
completely.  
 
The modifications could be performed in the wet lab without much preparation, as all of the genes are 
known. Another possibility would be to simulate the modification in a genome scale metabolic model. The 
study “Optimization of Lipid Production with a Genome-Scale Model of Yarrowia lipolytica ” by Kavšček et al. 
has previously been mentioned in the thesis. The authors created a reliable and accurate genome scale met-
abolic model, which could be a useful too.  
 
Once the strain has been constructed, it should be cultivated in the optimised medium described in this 
study, to see whether the modifications have had the desired effect or not.  
 
7.3.2. Production of high value carotenoids  
Beta-carotene is the precursor to a range of other carotenoids, including astaxanthin and zeaxanthin which 
was described in the background. Both carotenoids are high value compounds. The market for astaxanthin 
as a colourant for farmed fish and birds is increasing (Kildegaard et al. 2017).  
 
 
Figure 47: Figure 3A from the article “ Engineering of Yarrowia lipolytica for production of astaxanthin” written by Kildegaard et al  
(Kildegaard et al. 2017).  
==PAGE:95==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 95 of 154 
 
The enzymatic steps needed in the conversion of beta-carotene into a wide range of other carotenoids are 
shown in Figure 47. Only one enzyme is needed to convert beta-carotene to zeaxanthin and two for the con-
version into astaxanthin. Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF would be a suitable host for heterologous production of 
these carotenoids as it has the highest titer of beta-carotene to date.  
 
7.3.3. Secretion of beta-carotene  
The beta-carotene produced by Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF is stored in the bodies. During recovery and purifi-
cation, the cells need to be disrupted in order to recover the beta-carotene disruption of the cells also re-
leases many impurities which need to be removed in the subsequent steps of the downstream process. A 
way to circumvent the cell disruption step is to recover the product directly from the broth, which requires 
the product to be secreted. Beta-carotene is a very hydrophobic and big molecule, it is therefore not straight 
forward to secrete it through a transporter in the cell membrane.  It is however possible to engineer Y. lipo-
lytica  to secrete FFA. In 2016 a research group, led by Dr. Rodrigo Ledesma-Amaro, published an article with 
two strategies for the secretion of FFA. Beta-carotene and FFA are very similar and it is therefore likely that 
one of the strategies can be used for the secretion of beta-carotene. The possible disadvantage to the secre-
tion is the expose of the beta-carotene to light and oxygen, which could cause degradation. If lipids are co-
secreted, the beta-carotene would be enclosed in the lipids an thereby protected.   
 
7.4. Simulated industrial set-up 
One of the scopes of phase 4, was to apply the knowledge gained in the previous phases on media optima to 
design a process in a 10L fermentor that could simulate industrial conditions. Due to technical difficulties, 
the scale-up had to be discarded. When a medium has been designed, which proved to be significantly bet-
ter than the base-line an industrial set-up should be tested out. It would also be beneficial to postpone this 
test until a strain with the above suggested modifications, has been constructed and compared to Y. lipolyt-
ica ob-CHCTEFCTEF.

Appendix 3: Example of calculation of mole-based carbon/nitrogen ratios 
 
The carbon/nitrogen ratios in this study, were based on moles of pure carbon and pure nitrogen instead of 
being based on the media components glucose monohydrate and ammonium sulphate, which do not con-
tain equal amounts of carbon and nitrogen. This examples is based on the standard media composition for 
CBS medium, which contains 20 g/L of glucose and 5 g/L of ammonium sulphate.  
Formula for the calculations:  
𝑛 [𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒] =
𝑚 [𝑔]
𝑀 [𝑔/𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒] 
 
Firstly, the amount of moles of ammonium sulphate in 5 g is calculated:  
𝑛(2ef)ghif =
5 𝑔
132.14 𝑔/𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒= 0.038 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 (𝑁𝐻p)a𝑆𝑂p  
The difference in MW between ammonium sulphate and pure nitrogen is accounted for, by calculation the 
fraction of ammonium sulphate, which is nitrogen:  
28 𝑔/𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒
132.14 𝑔/𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒∗100% = 21.19% 
The amount of moles of nitrogen in the 5 g of ammonium sulphate is then calculated and normalised to one 
atom: 
0.038 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 (𝑁𝐻p)a𝑆𝑂p ∗0,2119
2
= 0.004 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 𝑁 
 
The same calculations are performed for glucose:  
Firstly, the amount of moles of glucose in 20 g is calculated:  
𝑛stu =
20 𝑔
180 𝑔/𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒= 0.11 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 𝑔𝑙𝑐  
The difference in MW between glucose and pure carbon is accounted for, by calculation the fraction of glu-
cose, which is carbon: 
70 𝑔/𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒
180 𝑔/𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒∗100% = 38,9% 
The amount of moles of carbon in the 20 g of glucose is then calculated and normalised to one atom: 
0.11 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 𝑔𝑙𝑐∗0.389
6
= 0.0071 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 𝐶 
The last step is to calculate the ratio between the two comounds:  
0.0071 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 𝐶
0.004 𝑚𝑜𝑙𝑒 𝑁= 1.78 
The C/N ratio of standard CBS medium is therefore 1.78

Appendix 5: Conversion of CO2 percentage to accumulated CO2 and verification 
 
The described method is adapted from the PhD thesis “Development of scalable high throughput fermenta-
tion approaches for physiological characterisation of yeast and filamentous fungi” by Peter Boldsen Knudsen 
(Knudsen 2015). The method describes how to convert percentages of CO2 in the off-gas stream into accu-
mulated CO2 in g/L, which can be used for calculation of specific growth rates and yields.  
 
The percentages of CO244 and CO245 were summarised to yield the total CO2 percentage. The total CO2% 
was background corrected, based on the reference air measured by the MS and set to 0.04 %.  
For the equation below: background corrected CO2 in percentage was converted into a rate (symbol; Qi, 
unit; L/h). The first term converted CO2 into a decimal number. The second term converted decimal CO2 into 
a rate and the last modified the unit to litres per hour.  
 
𝑄) ^𝐿
ℎ_ = y𝐶𝑂a[%]
100
z ∗𝑎𝑖𝑟𝑓𝑙𝑜𝑤^ 𝐿
𝑚𝑖𝑛_ ∗60 ^𝑚𝑖𝑛
ℎ_ 
 
The unit of the rate Qi was converted to mmol/h. The term Vm is derived from the ideal gas law: 𝑉} ~

  where R is the gas constant, T is the temperature and P is the pressure. The term was the 
multiplied with 1000 to converted the unit from [mol/h] to [mmol/h]: 
 
𝑄) ^𝑚𝑚𝑜𝑙
ℎ

𝑄~𝐿
ℎ•
𝑉}
∗1000 
 
To determine the accumulated CO2 [g/L] the rate QCO2 [mmol/h] was partially integrated and stepwise sum-
marised. The result was converted from [mmol/h] to [g/L] by multiplication with the molecular weight of 
CO2. The reactor volume was accounted for. For batch cultivations, a fixed value was used. For fed-batch cul-
tivations, the increase in volume was added for each time point assuming that feeding was continuous.

Verification of the correlation between CO2, DW and OD600  
 
DW and OD600 are classic and verified methods for determination of growth rate in yeast cultivations. The 
use of accumulated CO2 is not widely acknowledged and therefore it was chosen to verify the use in this par-
ticular study for Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF. It is known that a linear correlation exists between dry weight 
measurements and OD600 measurements. The criterion for verification was a linear correlation of accumu-
lated CO2 to both dry weight and OD600 measurements. The verification was conducted on minimal media 
fermentations.  
 
 
Appendix-Figure 6: Correlation between DW, OD600 and acc. 
CO2 for a fermentation with Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF on CBS 
medium. The replicate shown is representative. DW and acc. 
CO2 plotted against OD600. R-squared values were 0.99 and 
0.996 respectively 
 
Appendix-Figure 7: Correlation between DW, OD600 and acc. 
CO2 for a fermentation with Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF on CBS 
medium. The replicate shown is representative. OD600 and acc. 
CO2 plotted against DW. R-squared values were 0.99 for both. 
 
For the two classical methods, the R2 value is 0.99 which means that the correlation between the two is sta-
tistically significant. The correlation between CO2 and the classical methods did also prove to be statistically 
significant.  
 
Appendix-Table 2: R2 values for triplicate fermentations with Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF in CBS medium. All values are within the 90% 
confidence interval and 8/9 are within the 95% confidence interval and thereby statistically significant.

Appendix 6A and 6B 
Appendix 6A: Determination of specific growth rates from experimental data 
 
Specific growth rate determined from OD600 data 
OD600 values were plotted against time (orange line) and the exponential phase was identified (blue line). An 
exponential trend line was fitted to the data points from the exponential phase. The exponent in the equa-
tion for the trendline is the specific growth rate. In the case shown in Appendix-Figure 8, the specific growth 
rate is 0,162 h-1 and the R-squared value is 0.9978. 
 
Specific growth rate determined from dry weight and accumulated CO2  
The specific growth rates from dry weight and accumulated CO2 are determined in the same way.  
DW values or accumulated CO2 values in [g/L] were log-transformed, using the “LN” function in Microsoft 
Excel, and plotted against the cultivation time (orange line). A linear trend-line was fitted to the data points 
in the exponential phase (blue line). The slope of that trend-line is equal to the specific growth rate of the 
organism. For dry weight measurements; Appendix-Figure 8 shows a specific growth rate of 0.147 h-1. For 
CO2 measurements; Appendix-Figure 8  shows a specific growth rate of 0.151 h-1.  
 
Determining specific growth rate (µ) from LSU data 
The LSU data points for the exponential phase were plotted against cultivation time and a linear trendline 
was fitted. The exponent in the equation for that trend-line is the specific growth rate. The example shown 
in Appendix-Figure 8 is for a cultivation in CBS-glucose medium with a pH of 6. The specific growth rate was 
determined to be 0.168 h-1 with a R2 value of 0.9992.

Appendix 7: Statistical calculations and examples 
 
Statistical tools will be used in this study to compare Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF to Y. lipolytica, compare val-
ues in optimisation studies and finally to describe the consistency of triplicate data.  
 
Mean 
The mean is the average value in a dataset. It does not account for the variance of the data. In statistics the 
mean is denoted 𝑥̅. The mean is calculation using the following formula:  
𝑥̅ =
∑𝑥
𝑛 
Where:  
x is an observation, e.g. a specific growth rate 
n is the number of observations in the dataset 
 
Standard deviation (SD)  
The standard deviation is often used to describe how concentrated, or spread, the data is around the mean. 
It describes the typical distance from data point to the mean. A large standard deviation describes that the 
data is very spread out, whereas a small standard deviation describes that the data is concentrated around 
the mean. The standard deviation has the same unit as the values it describes. Standard deviation is calcu-
lated using the following formula: 
 𝑆𝐷= –∑(𝑥−𝑥̅)a
𝑛−1
 
 
Where:  
x is  an observation 
𝑥̅ is the mean  
n is the number of observations 
 
Plots for triplicate means, for instance cultivations will be equipped with error bars signifying one standard 
deviation on each side of the mean to visualise the spread in the underlying data.  
 
Example of mean and SD 
For a fermentation with Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF in standard CBS-glucose medium, the following specific 
growth rates were reported: 0.168 h-1 (OD600), 0.154 h-1 (DW) and 0.151 h-1 (accumulated CO2).  
The mean is:  
𝑥̅ = (0.168 ℎV- + 0.154 ℎV- + 0.151 ℎV-)
3
= 0.157ℎV- 
 
The standard deviation can be calculated as well:  
 
𝑆𝐷= –(0.168ℎV- −0.157ℎV-)a + (0.154ℎV- −0.157ℎV-)a(0.151ℎV- −0.157ℎV-)a
3 −1
= 0.008 
 
==PAGE:118==
Spring 2018 
Optimization of Beta-carotene production by  
Irene Hjorth Jacobsen 
 
Yarrowia lipolytica   
Page 118 of 154 
 
The answer is therefore: 0.157±0.008 h-1 
 
Confidence intervals (CI) 
Confidence intervals describe probability. A confidence interval is based on the observed data and will de-
scribe whether an unknown observation lies within the population or not at a certain confidence level. The 
confidence level is set by the user, but 95% is often used as it is described as statistically significant.  
The confidence interval is calculated from the following formula, when the mean and standard deviation is 
known. The mean plus the standard deviation is the upper CI and the mean minus the CI is the lower CI: 
 
𝐶𝐼= 𝑥̅ ± 𝑍∗𝑆𝐷
√𝑛 
Where:  
𝑥̅ is the mean for the population 
n is the number of observations 
SD is the standard deviation for the population 
Z* is a table value that is based on the confidence level, for the 95% confidence level Z* is 1.96 
 
In this study Cis will be used for visualisation in comparisons, to illustrate whether means differ or not. Ap-
pendix-Figure 13 describes the three possible situations and what can be drawn from them 
 
 
 
 
Confidence intervals overlap and en-
compass the means 
Confidence intervals overlap, the 
means are not encompassed in the 
overlap 
No overlap in confidence intervals 
Conclusion:  
Strong evidence that the populations 
do not differ 
Conclusion:  
No strong evidence to either side 
Conclusion:  
Strong evidence that the populations 
do differ

Appendix 8A and 8B 
Appendix 8A: Calculation of yields based on experimental data 
 
Yields were calculated in two ways in this study, based on the data type.  
 
Yields based on data from 1L batch fermentations 
In the fermenter experiments multiples samples were taken, which means that the yields can be based on 
rates, as described. A plot was made, where the x-axis was the basis – in this case the concentration of glyc-
erol. The concentration of the products were plotted on the y-axis. The plot is shown in Appendix-Figure 14 
To each a graph a linear trendline was fitted, the slope of that trendline is the yield of that product in rela-
tion to the basis. The yields are shown in red 
 
 
Appendix-Figure 14: Example of yield calculations based on plotted experimental data 
Yields based on data from micro-fermentations 
Multiple data points could not be obtained for micro-fermentations due to the small volume in the wells of 
the FlowerPlate™. Yields were therefore based on single measurements of beta-carotene and biomass, un-
der the assumption that all substrate had been consumed.  
 
Example:  
In a micro-fermentation with Y. lipolytica ob-CHCTEFCTEF in CBS-glucose at pH 5, 3.85 g of biomass was pro-
duced from 20 g glucose.

Appendix: 10 R-script for initial treatment of BioLector® I data 
 
#Script for transforming BioLector output data 
#Required packages are loaded into the current session. 
library(htmf) 
library(RColorBrewer) 
library(MASS) 
#Set working directory to location of raw data files 
setwd("~/Location/Folder/Sub-location/") 
#Show files in working directory 
dir() 
#csvFiles is the output file from the BioLector in csv format 
csvFiles = "BioLector_data_output.csv" 
#desfiles is a table with information on each well. 
desFiles = "Design_table.txt" 
#Outfiles are the new files created by the script, that contains the 
processed data. 
#One file is created for each filter in the BioLector; e.g. Biomass 
and RFP 
outfile1 = "Outfile_Biomass.txt" 
outfile2 = "Outfile_RFP.txt" 
#Defining values for baseline and outlier correction 
baselineThr <- 3 
outliersThr <- 5 
pr = read_biolector2(file=csvFiles, designfile = desFiles) 
pInt <- plate_intervals(pr) 
print(pInt) 
#The following determines the time window for the output data 
#Defining the end time-point (in hours) 
tMax = 72 
#Trim data to this time window (in hours) 
prs <- sliceByTimeIntervals(pr, intervals = list(c(2, tMax))) 
#Correct baseline and remove outliers 
prsn = plate_normalize(x=prs, filtersets="Biomass", spar=0.6, 
                            valign=TRUE, targetTime=2, targetAmp=0.5, 
                            baseline=FALSE, baselineThr=baselineThr, 
                            outliers=TRUE, outliersThr=outliersThr) 
#Construct output file 
odat = plateRun2df(prs) 
biomass = odat[odat$type=="Biomass",] 
write.table(file=outfile1, biomass[,c(2:5,7:12)], quote=FALSE, 
sep="\t", row.names=FALSE, dec=",") 
write.table(file=outfile2, biomass[,c(2:5,7:12)], quote=FALSE, 
sep="\t", row.names=FALSE, dec=",")
